Accidental Unity and the Distinction of Accidents
Defines accidental unity and distinguishes two categories of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of accidental unity and the key distinction between accidents that necessarily accompany the quiddity and those that depend on existence.

Core Idea
Accidents of quiddity are inseparable; accidents of existence require actual existence.

The commentator begins by clarifying the scope of accidental unity—whether the predicate is essential or accidental—and then introduces a crucial distinction: some accidents belong to the quiddity itself, while others arise only after existence. This distinction is foundational for later discussions on attributes and their relation to essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of accidental unity
Note how the commentator expands 'predicated upon it' to include both essential and accidental predication, thus covering all types of accidental unity.
2
Identify the two types of accidents
Focus on the contrast: accidents of quiddity are present even without existence (as necessary concomitants), whereas accidents of existence require actual existence.
The author's example of parts unified in a single matter illustrates this.
3
Understand the looseness in phrasing
The commentator points out that the author's phrase 'or they inhere in a single matter' is imprecise because inherence belongs to the form, not the composite parts.
This shows the care required in theological language.

قوله: (أي محمول عليها سواء كان بالطبع أو لا ليشمل القسمين. ثم إن أريد بعروضها للماهية أنها كافية في عروضها بعد الوجود كانت هذه الأقسام للوازم، وإليه تشير عبارة المصنف، حيث فرق بين عوارض الماهية وبين عوارض الوجود بأنه لو فرض الخلو عنها لم تكن تلك الأجزاء الحاصلة بالقسمة متحدة حالة في مادة واحدة؛ لأن المادة واحدة عند القائل بها، سواء كانت الأجزاء متصلة أو لا، هذا، ثم في قوله أو تحل في مادة واحدة نوع مسامحة؛ لأن الحال فيها هو الصورة لا تلك الأجزاء المركبة من الهيولى والصورة، فليفهم . قوله : (بين تلك الكثرة وغيرها ينبغي أن يراد بالكثرة بعض أفراد الجنس، لا مجموعها وإلا لم يبق للغير معنى. المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس: في أقسام الواحد عارض يلحق الماهية من حيث هي هي)، أي مع قطع النظر عن هوياتها الخارجية )، وعن وجودها الذهني أيضاً؛ إذ لا مدخل في ذلك اللحوق لخصوصية شيء من الوجودين، بل لمطلق الوجود بأمر ذاتي أولى من الواحد بأمر عرضي، وهو أولى من الماهية تلك الماهية، بخلاف عوارض الوجود وسيصرح به الشارح قدس سره أيضاً فيما بعد بقوله : لأن البحث عما يلحق الماهية أنه من لوازمها من حيث هي هي إلخ. وإن أريد به أنها تعرض الماهية ولو لمدخلية أمر آخر كان كل واحد من الأقسام الثلاثة منقسماً إلى اللازم والمفارق، وهو ظاهر؛ لجواز أن يكون العروض في الوجود الخارجي والذهني، أو كليهما مشروطاً بأمر منفك عن الماهية وقوله : فأينما وجدت إلخ لا يقتضي انحصار عوارض الماهية في اللازمة على ما وهم لأن شمول الأمكنة لا يقتضي شمول الأزمنة واعلم أن الحصر بين الأقسام الثلاثة عقلي ؛ لأن العروض لا يمكن بدون وجود المعروض، فإما أن يكون في الوجود الخارجي فقط، أو في الذهني فقط، أو فيهما، واحتمال قسم آخر كأن يكون العروض باعتبار الوجودين معا، أو كان يكون العروض باعتبار خصوصية كل منهما لا باعتبار مطلق وهم منشؤه عدم التدبر والالتفات إلى ما يوهمه ظاهر العبارة. مر أن الماهية قوله : أي) مع قطع النظر إلخ المقصود من التفسير دفع ما يرد من أنه قد من حيث هي هي ليست إلا الماهية فكيف يمكن لحوق شيء لها، وحاصله أنه ليس المراد بالماهية من حيث هي هي الماهية مع قطع النظر عما عداها حتى عن هذه الحيثية، بل الماهية مع قطع النظر عن هوياتها الخارجية، ولما كان هذا القدر كافياً في الدفع اكتفى المصنف عليه، وأحال قطع النظر عن الوجود الذهني على المقابلة، وزاده الشارح قدس سره تصريحاً بما علم من المقابلة . قوله : (بل لمطلق الوجود أي بل المدخل في ذلك لمطلق الوجود أي وجود كان، كما يدل عليه قول . قوله : (بل لمطلق الوجود أي بل المدخل له ويؤيده ما قيل : اقتضاء الماهية لشيء واتصافها به من غير نظر إلى الوجود غير معقول، فإنه من المعلوم بالضرورة أن ما لا ثبوت له بوجه من الوجوه لا يتصف بثبوت شيء له فليس معنى لازم الماهية أنها متصفة به، سواء وجدت بأحد الوجودين أو لا بل معناه أنها أينما وجدت كانت متصفة به؛ إذ ليس لأحد الوجودين مدخل في الاقتضاء، بل المقتضى الماهية باعتبار مطلق وجودها قيل وفيه بحث، لأن ما مع العلة لا يجب أن يكون له دخل في العلية فإن ما يساوي العلة لا ينفك عنها. ولا دخل له في يرى أن الصورة المشخصة علة لتشخص الهيولى مع كون الهيولى علة لتشخص الصورة، ثم الاقتضاء مقدم بالذات على الاتصاف، فلا يلزم من عدم انفكاك الماهية المتصفة بلوازمها عن الوجود المدخلية في العلية والاقتضاء اللهم إلا أن يقال : لو لم يكن للوجود دخل في الاقتضاء لصح الاتصاف مع قطع النظر عن الوجود ؛ لأن هذا الاتصاف حينئذ مقتضى الذات، العلية الا
His saying: (i.e., predicated upon it, whether essentially or not, to include the two divisions). Then if it is intended by its being an accident of the quiddity that it is sufficient in its occurrence after existence, then these divisions are of the necessary concomitants (al-lawazim). And to that refers the author's expression, where he distinguished between the accidents of quiddity ('awarid al-mahiyya) and the accidents of existence ('awarid al-wujud) by saying: If absence from them were assumed, those parts resulting from division would not be unified, existing in a single matter, because the matter is one according to the one who affirms it, whether the parts are connected or not. This, then in his saying 'or they inhere in a single matter', there is a kind of looseness, because the inheren is the form, not those parts composed of hyle and form, so understand. His saying: (between that many and others) it should be understood by 'many' some individuals of the genus, not the totality thereof, otherwise there remains no meaning for 'others'. The fourth observatory — the fifth intention: on the divisions of the one. An accident that attaches to the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself (i.e., disregarding its external identities, and also its mental existence), for there is no role in that attachment for the specificity of either of the two existences, but rather for absolute existence. By an intrinsic matter it is more fitting than the one by an accidental matter, and it is more fitting than the quiddity itself. Contrary to the accidents of existence, and the commentator (may God sanctify his secret) will also explicitly state later in his saying: 'Because the investigation into what attaches to the quiddity is that it is among its necessary concomitants inasmuch as it is itself, etc.' And if it is intended by it that it occurs to the quiddity even with the involvement of another matter, then each of the three divisions is divided into the necessary concomitant (al-lazim) and the separable (al-mufariq), and it is apparent, because it is permissible that the occurrence be in external or mental existence, or both, conditioned by something separable from the quiddity. And his saying: 'Wherever it exists, etc.' does not necessitate the confinement of the accidents of quiddity to the necessary concomitants, as some have imagined, because the universality of places does not necessitate the universality of times. And know that the enumeration among the three divisions is rational, because occurrence cannot be without the existence of the subject of occurrence; so either it is in external existence only, or in mental existence only, or in both. The possibility of another division, such as occurrence with regard to both existences together, or occurrence with regard to the specificity of each of them not with regard to the absolute, is a delusion whose source is lack of reflection and attention to what the apparent expression might suggest. It was said that the quiddity... His saying: (i.e., disregarding, etc.) The intent of the interpretation is to repel what is objected: that the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself is nothing but the quiddity, so how can something attach to it? The summary is that what is meant by 'the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself' is not the quiddity disregarding everything else, even this aspect, but rather the quiddity disregarding its external identities. And since this much is sufficient for repelling, the author sufficed with it, and referred disregarding mental existence to the contrast. The commentator (may God sanctify his secret) added explicit mention of what is known from the contrast. His saying: (but rather for absolute existence) i.e., rather the entrance [cause] for that is absolute existence, i.e., any existence whatever, as his saying indicates. His saying: (but rather for absolute existence) i.e., rather the entrance is for it, and it is supported by what is said: The quiddity's requirement (iqtida') of something and its being qualified by it without regard to existence is inconceivable; for it is known by necessity that what has no existence in any respect cannot be qualified with the affirmation of something for it. So the meaning of 'necessary concomitant of the quiddity' is not that it is qualified by it whether it exists in either of the two existences or not; rather its meaning is that wherever it exists, it is qualified by it. For neither of the two existences has any role in the requirement, but rather the requirer is the quiddity with regard to its absolute existence. It is said, and in it there is discussion, because what is together with the cause does not necessarily have a role in causality; indeed, what equals the cause is not separate from it. And it has no role in... you see that the particularizing form is the cause for the particularization of hyle, while the hyle is the cause for the particularization of the form. Then the requirement is prior by essence to qualification, so the non-separation of the quiddity qualified by its necessary concomitants from existence does not necessitate the causality and the requirement. Unless it is said: If existence had no role in the requirement, then qualification would be valid with disregard for existence, because this qualification would then be a requirement of the essence, but the causality...
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on accidental unity (al-wahid bi'l-'arad) and a detailed analysis of the distinction between essential and accidental attributes ('awarid al-mahiyya vs 'awarid al-wujud). The commentator begins by explaining the phrase 'i.e., predicated upon it, whether essentially or not' to include both types of accidental unity. He then says that if we take 'occurrence to the quiddity' to mean that the quiddity is sufficient for its occurrence after existence, then these divisions (of the one) fall under necessary concomitants (lawazim). He references the author's distinction between accidents of quiddity and accidents of existence, giving an example about parts resulting from division being unified in a single matter, and points out a looseness in the author's phrasing regarding inherence.

Next, he clarifies that 'between that many and others' means some individuals of the genus, not all. Then the passage transitions to discuss the accidental attribute that attaches to the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself, disregarding both external and mental existence. The point is that the attachment does not depend on a specific existence but on absolute existence. The commentator distinguishes this from accidents of existence and cites the author's later statement about necessary concomitants.

The commentator then distinguishes two interpretations. If attaching accidents are considered without any external factor, they are necessary concomitants. If an external factor can intervene, then each of the three divisions (external, mental, both) can be further divided into necessary and separable. He refutes the idea that 'wherever it exists' implies only concomitants, because spatial universality does not imply temporal universality. The threefold division (external, mental, both) is rational, and any other division is a delusion.

Finally, the commentator addresses an objection: How can something attach to the quiddity in itself? The answer is that 'in itself' does not mean disregarding everything, but only disregarding external identities; mental existence is set aside by contrast. He then engages with a deeper discussion about iqtida' (requirement) and 'illah (causality). The argument is that the quiddity's requirement of its necessary concomitants is in respect of its absolute existence, not specific existence. Some object that if existence has no role, qualification would be valid without existence, but the commentator shows that this does not follow because what accompanies a cause may not itself be a cause. He gives the example of form and hyle mutually causing each other's particularization. The requirement is prior to qualification, so the inseparability of the qualified quiddity from existence does not entail causality. The passage ends mid-sentence with a counter-argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'accidental unity' only covers accidental predication, not essential.
Correction: The commentator explicitly says the definition includes both 'whether essentially or not', so both essential and accidental predication are considered.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what condition makes an accident a 'necessary concomitant' of the quiddity?
A) The quiddity is sufficient for its occurrence after existence
B) The accident occurs only in external existence
C) The accident is separable from the quiddity
D) The accident is predicated essentially

Key Takeaway
Accidental unity includes both essential and accidental predication, and the key distinction is whether an accident necessarily accompanies the quiddity or only appears after existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that some attributes are inseparable from a quiddity. Think of your own character: which of your traits do you treat as inseparable from who you are? Are you certain they are essential, or could they be accidents of existence that you have attached to your identity?
?
The author uses the example of parts not being unified without certain accidents. In your spiritual life, what 'accidents' (practices or states) do you think are necessary for your unity with Allah? Are you willing to let go of those that are merely accidental?

Logic and Technical Terms
advanced
10 min
Logical Division of Accidents: Concomitant, Separable, and the Three Existences
Explains the logical division of accidents into necessary/separable and the three existential modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational division of accidents into necessary and separable, and the threefold classification based on existence (external, mental, or both).

Core Idea
Accidents are divided by existence: external, mental, or both; each can be necessary or separable.

After establishing the basic distinction between accidents of quiddity and existence, the commentator now presents a more detailed logical division. He explains that any accident can be either a necessary concomitant (lazim) or separable (mufariq), and that the occurrence of an accident must be in one of three realms: external existence, mental existence, or both. This division is rational and exhaustive.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two interpretations of 'occurrence to the quiddity'
First, if occurrence does not involve an external factor, it is a necessary concomitant. Second, if an external factor can intervene, each division further splits into necessary and separable.
This distinction is crucial for understanding later debates on attributes.
2
Learn the threefold division of existence
The commentator says occurrence must be in external existence only, mental existence only, or both. No other possibility exists; any other is a delusion.
This is a rational (aqli) enumeration.
3
Refute the misconception about 'wherever it exists'
Some think the phrase implies only necessary concomitants, but spatial universality does not imply temporal universality.
The commentator uses a logical rebuttal.
4
Examine the objection and reply on iqtida'
The final part addresses whether existence has a role in the quiddity's requirement of its concomitants. The commentator argues that the quiddity's requirement is by absolute existence, not specific existence, and shows that what accompanies a cause may not itself be a cause.
Watch for the incomplete argument at the end due to cut-off text.

Source Text

His saying: (i.e., predicated upon it, whether essentially or not, to include the two divisions). Then if it is intended by its being an accident of the quiddity that it is sufficient in its occurrence after existence, then these divisions are of the necessary concomitants (al-lawazim). And to that refers the author's expression, where he distinguished between the accidents of quiddity ('awarid al-mahiyya) and the accidents of existence ('awarid al-wujud) by saying: If absence from them were assumed, those parts resulting from division would not be unified, existing in a single matter, because the matter is one according to the one who affirms it, whether the parts are connected or not. This, then in his saying 'or they inhere in a single matter', there is a kind of looseness, because the inheren is the form, not those parts composed of hyle and form, so understand. His saying: (between that many and others) it should be understood by 'many' some individuals of the genus, not the totality thereof, otherwise there remains no meaning for 'others'. The fourth observatory — the fifth intention: on the divisions of the one. An accident that attaches to the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself (i.e., disregarding its external identities, and also its mental existence), for there is no role in that attachment for the specificity of either of the two existences, but rather for absolute existence. By an intrinsic matter it is more fitting than the one by an accidental matter, and it is more fitting than the quiddity itself. Contrary to the accidents of existence, and the commentator (may God sanctify his secret) will also explicitly state later in his saying: 'Because the investigation into what attaches to the quiddity is that it is among its necessary concomitants inasmuch as it is itself, etc.' And if it is intended by it that it occurs to the quiddity even with the involvement of another matter, then each of the three divisions is divided into the necessary concomitant (al-lazim) and the separable (al-mufariq), and it is apparent, because it is permissible that the occurrence be in external or mental existence, or both, conditioned by something separable from the quiddity. And his saying: 'Wherever it exists, etc.' does not necessitate the confinement of the accidents of quiddity to the necessary concomitants, as some have imagined, because the universality of places does not necessitate the universality of times. And know that the enumeration among the three divisions is rational, because occurrence cannot be without the existence of the subject of occurrence; so either it is in external existence only, or in mental existence only, or in both. The possibility of another division, such as occurrence with regard to both existences together, or occurrence with regard to the specificity of each of them not with regard to the absolute, is a delusion whose source is lack of reflection and attention to what the apparent expression might suggest. It was said that the quiddity... His saying: (i.e., disregarding, etc.) The intent of the interpretation is to repel what is objected: that the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself is nothing but the quiddity, so how can something attach to it? The summary is that what is meant by 'the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself' is not the quiddity disregarding everything else, even this aspect, but rather the quiddity disregarding its external identities. And since this much is sufficient for repelling, the author sufficed with it, and referred disregarding mental existence to the contrast. The commentator (may God sanctify his secret) added explicit mention of what is known from the contrast. His saying: (but rather for absolute existence) i.e., rather the entrance [cause] for that is absolute existence, i.e., any existence whatever, as his saying indicates. His saying: (but rather for absolute existence) i.e., rather the entrance is for it, and it is supported by what is said: The quiddity's requirement (iqtida') of something and its being qualified by it without regard to existence is inconceivable; for it is known by necessity that what has no existence in any respect cannot be qualified with the affirmation of something for it. So the meaning of 'necessary concomitant of the quiddity' is not that it is qualified by it whether it exists in either of the two existences or not; rather its meaning is that wherever it exists, it is qualified by it. For neither of the two existences has any role in the requirement, but rather the requirer is the quiddity with regard to its absolute existence. It is said, and in it there is discussion, because what is together with the cause does not necessarily have a role in causality; indeed, what equals the cause is not separate from it. And it has no role in... you see that the particularizing form is the cause for the particularization of hyle, while the hyle is the cause for the particularization of the form. Then the requirement is prior by essence to qualification, so the non-separation of the quiddity qualified by its necessary concomitants from existence does not necessitate the causality and the requirement. Unless it is said: If existence had no role in the requirement, then qualification would be valid with disregard for existence, because this qualification would then be a requirement of the essence, but the causality...
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on accidental unity (al-wahid bi'l-'arad) and a detailed analysis of the distinction between essential and accidental attributes ('awarid al-mahiyya vs 'awarid al-wujud). The commentator begins by explaining the phrase 'i.e., predicated upon it, whether essentially or not' to include both types of accidental unity. He then says that if we take 'occurrence to the quiddity' to mean that the quiddity is sufficient for its occurrence after existence, then these divisions (of the one) fall under necessary concomitants (lawazim). He references the author's distinction between accidents of quiddity and accidents of existence, giving an example about parts resulting from division being unified in a single matter, and points out a looseness in the author's phrasing regarding inherence.

Next, he clarifies that 'between that many and others' means some individuals of the genus, not all. Then the passage transitions to discuss the accidental attribute that attaches to the quiddity inasmuch as it is itself, disregarding both external and mental existence. The point is that the attachment does not depend on a specific existence but on absolute existence. The commentator distinguishes this from accidents of existence and cites the author's later statement about necessary concomitants.

The commentator then distinguishes two interpretations. If attaching accidents are considered without any external factor, they are necessary concomitants. If an external factor can intervene, then each of the three divisions (external, mental, both) can be further divided into necessary and separable. He refutes the idea that 'wherever it exists' implies only concomitants, because spatial universality does not imply temporal universality. The threefold division (external, mental, both) is rational, and any other division is a delusion.

Finally, the commentator addresses an objection: How can something attach to the quiddity in itself? The answer is that 'in itself' does not mean disregarding everything, but only disregarding external identities; mental existence is set aside by contrast. He then engages with a deeper discussion about iqtida' (requirement) and 'illah (causality). The argument is that the quiddity's requirement of its necessary concomitants is in respect of its absolute existence, not specific existence. Some object that if existence has no role, qualification would be valid without existence, but the commentator shows that this does not follow because what accompanies a cause may not itself be a cause. He gives the example of form and hyle mutually causing each other's particularization. The requirement is prior to qualification, so the inseparability of the qualified quiddity from existence does not entail causality. The passage ends mid-sentence with a counter-argument.

المفارق

الوجود الذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'necessary concomitant of the quiddity' means the quiddity is qualified by it even when the quiddity has no existence at all.
Correction: The passage explains: 'ليس معنى لازم الماهية أنها متصفة به، سواء وجدت بأحد الوجودين أو لا بل معناه أنها أينما وجدت كانت متصفة به' – it means wherever it exists, it is qualified.

Checkpoint
How many rational divisions of accidents does the commentator identify based on existence?
A) Two: necessary and separable
B) Three: external, mental, and both
C) Four: essential, accidental, necessary, separable
D) Six: external necessary, external separable, etc.

Key Takeaway
Accidents are rationally divided based on existence into three categories (external, mental, both), and each can be either a necessary concomitant or separable depending on whether an external factor is involved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the quiddity's necessary concomitants are not caused by existence itself. Consider your own relationship with knowledge: Do you seek knowledge as a necessary part of your being, or do you treat it as an accidental addition? How would your pursuit of knowledge change if you realized it is a necessary concomitant of your created nature, irrespective of external validation? Take one concrete step today to pursue knowledge as an essential part of your soul, not as a mere adornment.
?
The commentator warns against a delusion: thinking there is a fourth division of existence. How many times have you invented unnecessary categories in your own understanding of faith, complicating the clear teachings of the Qur'an and Sunnah? Identify one such unnecessary distinction you have held—perhaps between 'heart knowledge' and 'mind knowledge'—and write a resolution to abandon it, relying on the simplicity of the truth.
?
The lesson teaches that 'wherever it exists' does not imply necessity in all places. Think of a characteristic you ascribe to yourself as 'always'—like patience or anger. Is that truly a necessary concomitant of your soul, or is it separable and dependent on circumstances? How can you align your self-assessment with the reality of your changing states, and what does that demand from you in terms of humility before Allah?

The True One and the Priority of Unity
Classification of the one and priority of true unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of the one into types and the reasoning behind the priority of the true one and its unity.

Core Idea
The true one that admits no division is the most deserving of being called one.

In this lesson, the author lays out the fundamental categories of 'the one' (al-wahid). He distinguishes between the individual one that cannot be divided at all—the true one—and other ones that can be divided in some way. He then argues that the true one and its associated unity are prior to other types. The commentary explains why unity is inseparable from the true one, making its unity superior.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of the one
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the different types: the individual one (al-wahid al-shakhsi) that accepts no division at all is called the true one. This is contrasted with what accepts division in some respect.
The passage also mentions one by connection and one by aggregation.
2
Identify the three priority statements
Identify the three priority claims: (1) the true one is prior to what accepts division; (2) the unity that belongs to the true one is prior to other unities; (3) the one by connection is prior to the one by aggregation.
Think about why connection is more unified than aggregation.
3
Understand why unity of the true one is prior
Read the commentary explaining that unity cannot be conceived separately from the true one, unlike the point and the separable, where separation can be conceived even if impossible.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس : في أقسام الواحد

الواحد بالنسبة ثم الواحد الشخصي إن لم يقبل انقساماً أصلاً لا بحسب الأجزاء المقدارية، ولا بحسب غيرها محمولة كانت أو غير محمولة، وهو المسمى بالواحد الحقيقي أولى مما يقبل الانقسام بوجه ما. والوحدة التي من أقسام الواحد الحقيقي أولى من غيرها. والواحد بالاتصال أولى من الواحد بالاجتماع. وإذا كانت مقولية الوحدة

قوله : ( والوحدة التي من أقسام إلخ ) لأنه لا يمكن تصور انفكاك الوحدة عنها، فالتصور والمتصور فيها كلاهما محالان بخلاف القسمين الباقيين أعني النقطة والمفارق، فإنه يمكن تصور انفكاك الوحدة عنهما، وإن كان التصور محالاً. وما قاله الشارح قدس سره في حواشي شرح التجريد من كون الواجب تعالى الذي هو فرد من المفارق لعدم قبوله القسمة إلى الأجزاء أصلاً أولى بالوحدة من الوحدة الشخصية فمبني على كون التشخص جزءاً منها، كما صرح به فيها، فلا تدافع بين الكلامين ولا احتياج إلى تكلف بارد بأن يحمل الواحد الحقيقي في قوله : وهو المسمى بالواحد الحقيقي على معنى ما لا يقبل الانقسام إلى الأجزاء أصلاً، وفي قوله : من أقسام الواحد الحقيقي على معنى ما لا يقبل الانقسام إلى الأجزاء المقدارية، وأن يصرف قوله : أصلاً فيما تقدم عن معناه الظاهر إلى معنى لا حقيقة ولا حساً.

قوله : ( وإذا كانت مقولية إلخ ) لا يخفى أن اللازم مما ذكر كون الواحد مقولاً على ما تحته بالتشكيك والمقصود كون الوحدة بالنسبة إلى أفرادها كذلك قدر الشارح قدس سره الشرطية وجعل ضمير فيكون راجعاً إلى الوحدات لكن الكلام في لزوم كون الوحدة كذلك مما تقدم. ووجه اللزوم أنه لما كان الواحد باعتبار معنى الوحدة مقولاً بالتشكيك على أفراده، كان حصول الوحدة في معروضاتها مختلفة، فكان بعض أفراد الوحدة أولى بالوحدة من البعض الآخر أيضاً، فتدبر. وأنت خبير بأن الاقتضاء أمر ثبوتي فالاتصاف به يقتضي أحد الوجودين وبه يتم الكلام فتأمل.
The Fourth Observatory - Fifth Goal: On the Divisions of the One

The one with respect (then the individual one if it does not accept division at all, neither according to quantitative parts nor according to other parts, whether predicate or non-predicate, and it is called the true one) is more prior than what accepts division in some respect. And the unity which is among the divisions of the true one is more prior than others. And the one by connection is more prior than the one by aggregation. And if the predicability of unity…

His statement: (And the unity which is among the divisions, etc.) because it is impossible to conceive the separation of unity from them, so both the conception and the conceived are impossible, unlike the remaining two divisions, namely the point and the separable, for it is possible to conceive the separation of unity from them, even if the conception is impossible. And what the commentator said in his glosses on Sharh al-Tajrid about the Necessary Being, Who is an individual of the separable in not accepting division into parts at all, being more prior in unity than individual unity, is based on individuation being a part of it, as he explicitly stated there. So there is no contradiction between the two statements, nor is there need for any forced interpretation by taking the 'true one' in his statement 'and it is called the true one' to mean what does not accept division into parts at all, and in his statement 'the divisions of the true one' to mean what does not accept division into quantitative parts, and by diverting his word 'at all' from its apparent meaning to a meaning of neither in reality nor in sensation.

His statement: (And if the predicability, etc.) It is not hidden that the necessary consequence of what was mentioned is that the one is predicated on its instances by way of ambiguity, and the intent is that unity with respect to its individuals is likewise. The commentator expressed the conditional and made the pronoun 'it' refer to the unities, but the discussion is about the necessity of unity being such from what preceded. And the aspect of necessity is that since the one, by virtue of the meaning of unity, is predicated by ambiguity on its individuals, the realization of unity in its subjects is different, so some individuals of unity are more prior in unity than others also, so reflect. And you are aware that the requirement is a positive matter, so the description by it necessitates one of the two existences, and with that the speech is completed, so contemplate.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage discusses the divisions of the one (الواحد) and argues for the priority of certain types. It begins by comparing different kinds of the one: the individual one that admits no division at all (true one) is more prior than what admits division in some way. Then unity (الوحدة) as a division of the true one is more prior than other divisions. The one by connection (بالاتصال) is more prior than the one by aggregation (بالاجتماع). The text then provides a commentary on the phrase 'the unity which is among the divisions of the true one is more prior than others'. The commentator explains that unity cannot be conceived separately from the true one, unlike the point and the separable (المفارق), which can be conceptually separated even if not in reality. He then addresses a potential contradiction with the commentator's own gloss on Sharh al-Tajrid, where the Necessary Being (the highest separable) is said to be more prior in unity than individual unity. The commentator resolves this by noting that in that gloss, individuation (التشخص) is considered part of unity, so no contradiction exists; forced reconciliations are unnecessary. Finally, the passage discusses the predicability (مقولية) of unity. It explains that the one is predicated on its instances by way of ambiguity (tashkik), meaning that different instances have the concept in differing degrees of priority. Consequently, unity itself is also predicated ambiguously: some unities are more truly unity than others. The commentary concludes with a remark about requirement (اقتضاء) being positive, implying that description by it requires one of the two existences.

Key Terms
الواحد
الواحد الحقيقي
الوحدة
الاتصال
الاجتماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the unity belonging to the true one more prior than other unities?
A) Because the true one is indivisible.
B) Because unity cannot be conceived separately from the true one.
C) Because the point and separable are not real.
D) Because the commentator said so.

Key Takeaway
The true one, which admits no division, and its inseparable unity are the most prior forms of oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the true one is the most deserving of being called one, then what does it mean when you say 'Allah is One'? Does your heart truly affirm that He admits no division, no parts, and no separation in His essence? How would your worship change if you began each prayer with this absolute unity in mind?
?
You often consider your own identity as a single self, but you change and are divided by thoughts, desires, and moods. In light of this lesson, what does it mean for you to seek unity with your Creator? Is the oneness you seek a mere aggregation of good deeds, or a connection that leaves no room for division?
?
The passage says unity of the true one cannot even be conceived separately. Yet you daily conceive of Allah's attributes as if they are distinct from His essence. How can you train your mind to see His mercy, power, and wisdom as one undivided reality? What step will you take today to remove the mental division you create when you think of Him?

Resolving a Contradiction and the Ambiguous Predication of Unity
Resolution of contradiction and discussion on ambiguous predication of unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an apparent contradiction in the priority of unity is resolved and how unity is predicated by ambiguity (tashkik).

Core Idea
Unity is predicated on its individuals by way of ambiguity, and apparent contradictions in priority are resolved by context.

In this lesson, the author addresses a potential contradiction between two statements about priority of unity. He explains that the Necessary Being's priority is based on a different definition of 'true one' that includes individuation. Then he discusses how unity is predicated by ambiguity, meaning some unities are more truly unity than others. This clarifies the hierarchical nature of oneness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the apparent contradiction
Read the passage about the commentator's earlier statement that the Necessary Being is more prior in unity than individual unity. This seems to contradict the previous lesson's claim that individual unity is most prior.
Note: The Necessary Being is an individual of the separable (al-mufariq).
2
Follow the resolution
The commentator resolves the contradiction by noting that in the earlier gloss, 'true one' was defined without considering individuation (al-tashakhkhus), while here individuation is included. There is no need for forced reinterpretation.
The passage explicitly rejects two forced interpretations.
3
Learn about tashkik (ambiguous predication)
The second part discusses how the one is predicated on its instances by way of ambiguity (tashkik). This means that different instances have the concept in varying degrees. Consequently, unity itself is also predicated ambiguously: some unities are more perfect than others.
The final remark about requirement (iqtida') being a positive matter that necessitates one of two existences completes the argument.

What is the resolution to the apparent contradiction about the priority of unity?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever insisted on a particular interpretation of a theological text to avoid admitting a contradiction, even when a more coherent understanding was available? The author here warns against 'forced interpretations' (takalluf barid). Where in your own beliefs might you be guilty of such forced reasoning? Will you commit to seeking contextual resolutions instead?
?
The passage shows that context (inclusion or exclusion of individuation) resolves an apparent contradiction. Think of a time you misunderstood someone's statement because you ignored the context. How does this lesson call you to be more careful in reading religious texts? What practical steps will you take to avoid such misunderstandings in your study of aqeedah?
?
'Some unities are more prior than others.' In your spiritual life, you claim God's oneness, but do you treat His unity as abstract and distant, or as a living reality that demands your whole heart? How does the gradation of unity challenge you to deepen your understanding of tawhid beyond a mere statement? What will you do today to make God's oneness more real in your actions?

The Diversity of Unity: Likeness and Generality
Al-Jurjani begins classification of unity into types based on substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that unity is diversified into types based on the subject it inheres in, and that the terms 'mumathala' and 'mujanasa' express agreement in species and genus respectively.

Core Idea
Unity takes different names depending on what it describes.

Al-Jurjani introduces the important principle that the concept of unity is not monolithic; it varies according to the thing it qualifies. To make discussion precise, each type of unity has a specific term. In this passage, we learn the first two: mumathala (likeness) for agreement in species, and mujanasa (generality) for agreement in genus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note that the passage is incomplete; further types follow in the original.
2
Understand the principle
Grasp that unity is not a single concept but diversified according to its substrate, and each type has a conventional name.
3
Identify the two terms
Find 'mumathala' and 'mujanasa' in the text. Notice how each is defined: one for agreement in specific quiddity (species), the other for agreement in genus.
4
Reflect on terminology
Ask yourself why precise terminology is crucial in kalam. These terms allow clear expression of theological concepts.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد السادس : الوحدة تتنوع ولكل نوع اسم ( الوحدة تتنوع) أنواعاً (بحسب ما فيه ولكل نوع منها (اسم) يخصه بحسب الاصطلاح تسهيلا للتعبير عنها، ففي النوع مماثلة)، فإذا قيل: هما متماثلان كان معناه أنهما متفقان في الماهية النوعية، وفي الجنس مجانسة، وفي
The Fourth Observation - Sixth Section: Unity is diversified and every type has a name. Unity is diversified into types according to what it inheres in, and for each type there is a name that belongs to it according to terminology to facilitate expression about it. So in the type there is mumathala (likeness); so when it is said 'they are similar', its meaning is that they agree in specific quiddity. And in the genus there is mujanasa (generality), and in
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the sixth section, which classifies unity according to the subject it inheres in and assigns specific terms. It states that unity is divided into types based on its substrate, and each type has a conventional name to make expression easier. The first two types mentioned are: (1) when two things agree in species, the relation is called mumathala (likeness), and the statement 'they are similar' means they share the same specific quiddity; (2) when they agree in genus, the relation is called mujanasa (generality). The passage breaks off before listing further types (such as mushakala for relation in accidents, etc.). The commentary that immediately follows in the OCR belongs to the previous section and is not part of this lesson.

What does 'mumathala' express according to the passage?
A) Agreement in genus.
B) Agreement in specific quiddity.
C) Agreement in a common accident.
D) Agreement in existence.

Key Takeaway
The terms 'mumathala' and 'mujanasa' are conventional names for unity in species and genus, facilitating precise theological discourse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say that something is 'like' something else, do you ever stop to consider whether that likeness is at the level of species or genus? How might this distinction affect your understanding of Allah's uniqueness and the creation's diversity? Will you commit to being more precise in your comparisons from now on?
?
Scholars coined specific terms like 'mumathala' and 'mujanasa' to safeguard precise expression. In your daily speech, where are you vague about similarity that could lead to mistaken beliefs about Allah or His creation? What will you do to correct that?

Equivocal Unity: The Principle of Diversification
Sanusi explains the equivocal nature of unity and its consequence for judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how unity is diversified through equivocation (tashkik) and why its units can differ in reality while sharing a common concept, analogous to existence.

Core Idea
Unity is not a single reality; its types differ like existences differ.

Before exploring the specific types of unity, Sanusi lays down a foundational principle: the units of unity are related by equivocation (tashkik). This means they share a common concept but differ in reality, just as particular existences share existence but differ in essence. This principle allows unity to vary in its judgments and avoids pitfalls like infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on equivocal unity
Start by reading the Arabic passage from the beginning up to 'فيجوز أن يبنى على ذلك ويقال'. Let the author's argument about equivocity sink in before analyzing.
Notice the analogy with existence: different realities share the accident of absolute existence.
2
Understand the analogy with existence
Sanusi compares unity units to particular existences: just as existences differ in reality but share the concept of existence, unity units differ in reality but share the concept of absolute unity.
This analogy clarifies how equivocity works: a common accident with differing realities.
3
Recognize the key consequence
The main point is that units of unity do not necessarily share the same judgment. This flexibility is crucial for later discussions about unity being existential, mental, etc.
This consequence is stated explicitly: 'فلا يجب اشتراكها في الحكم'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد السادس : الوحدة تتنوع ولكل نوع اسم
على وحدات تلك الأقسام بالتشكيك ( فتكون تلك الوحدات مختلفة بالحقيقة)، متشاركة في هذا العارض الذي هو مفهوم الوحدة مطلقاً، على قياس اختلاف الوجودات الخاصة بالحقائق، مع الاشتراك في العارض الذي هو الوجود المطلق . ( فلا يجب ) حينئذ ( اشتراكها ) أي اشتراك الوحدات ( في الحكم)، فيجوز أن يبنى على ذلك ويقال : ( فمنها ما هو وجودي كالوحدة الاتصالية والاجتماعية على ما سيأتي، ( ومنها ما هو اعتباري محض فلا يلزم من وجودية الوحدة تسلسل في الأمور الموجودة؛ لجواز الانتهاء إلى وحدة اعتبارية، ولا يلزم من عدميتها في الجملة كونها اعتبارية على الإطلاق ، ( ومنها ما هو زائد ) على ماهية الواحد، كوحدة الإنسان مثلاً، ( ومنها ما هو نفس الماهية كوحدة الوحدة فإنها واحدة بذاتها، لا بوحدة زائدة عليها، ( ومنها ما هو جزؤها ) أي يجوز كونها جزءاً منها، وكذلك سائر الأحكام) فيقال مثلاً جاز كونها جوهراً في بعض، وعرضاً في بعض آخر، فتنبه له أي لما ذكرناه من جواز اختلاف الوحدات في الأحكام، فإنه ينفعك في مواضع متعددة.

قوله : ( فتكون تلك الوحدات إلخ) أي يجوز أن يكون كذلك.
قوله : ( ولا يلزم من عدميتها في الجملة أي باعتبار بعض أفرادها كونها اعتبارية باعتبار جميع الأفراد، بخلاف ما إذا كانت متحدة ،الماهية فإنه لا يجوز اختلاف أفرادها بالوجود والعدم، لما مر مراراً من أن كل ما من شأنه الوجود في الخارج لا يجوز الاتصاف به إلا بعد وجوده، فيه؛ كيلا يلزم السفسطة .
أقسام الواحد الحقيقي أولى من غيرها أنها أولى أقسام الواحد الحقيقي بالمعنى العام سوى الواجب تعالى بقرينة أنه صرح أولاً بأنه أولى من الكل فيؤول إلى ما ذكره في شرح التجريد، فتأمل .
قوله : ( فتكون تلك الوحدات مختلفة بالحقيقة) أي يجوز أن يكون كذلك، على ما مر من الشارح في بحث الوجود، وإنما فرع على التشكيك لأنه يظهر حينئذ . قوله : (جاز كونها جوهراً في بعض) أي ليس عرضية الوحدة في بعض مانع لجوهريتها في بعض آخر، لا أن جوهريتها في بعض جائز.
The Fourth Observatory - The Sixth Intention: Unity is diversified and each type has a name. (Proceeding) upon the units of those divisions by equivocation (tashkīk), (so those units are different in reality), sharing in this accident which is the concept of unity absolutely, analogous to the difference of particular existences in realities, with the sharing in the accident which is absolute existence. (So it is not necessary) then (their sharing) i.e., the sharing of units (in judgment), so it is permissible to build upon that and say: (Among them is what is existential, like connective and social unity as will come; among them is what is purely mental (i‘tibārī), so it does not necessitate from the existentiality of unity an infinite regress in existing things, because of the permissibility of ending at a mental unity; and it does not necessitate from its nonexistentiality in general that it is mental absolutely; among them is what is additional (zā'id) to the essence (māhiyya) of the one, like the unity of man for example; among them is what is the very essence, like the unity of unity, for it is one by itself, not by an additional unity; among them is what is its part) i.e., it is permissible for it to be a part of it, and likewise the rest of the judgments) so it is said, for example, it is permissible for it to be a substance in some and an accident in others. So be mindful of this, i.e., what we have mentioned about the permissibility of the difference of units in judgments, for it benefits you in many places. His saying: (So those units etc.) i.e., it is permissible for it to be like that. His saying: (And it does not necessitate from their non-existentiality in general) i.e., considering some of its individuals, that it is mental considering all individuals, unlike if they were united in essence, for then it is not permissible for its individuals to differ in existence and non-existence, as has been repeated many times that everything that is apt for existence in the external is not permissible to be described with it except after its existence in it, lest sophistry (safsata) follow. The divisions of the real one (al-wāḥid al-ḥaqīqī) are more deserving than others, that they are the most deserving divisions of the real one in the general sense except the Necessary Exalted, by indication that he first stated that it is more deserving than all, so it reverts to what he mentioned in Sharḥ al-Tajrīd, so ponder. His saying: (So those units are different in reality) i.e., it is permissible for it to be like that, as the commentator (al-shāriḥ) has passed in the discussion of existence, and he only branched upon equivocation because it appears then. His saying: (It is permissible for it to be a substance in some) i.e., the accidentalness (ʿaraḍiyya) of unity in some is not a preventer of its substantiality (jawhariyya) in others, not that its substantiality in some is permissible.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the different types of unity (al-wahda). It begins by stating that unity is diversified according to what it is in, and each type has a specific name. The author then discusses that these units (wihādāt) can be different in reality while sharing the accidental concept of absolute unity, analogous to the difference among particular existences while sharing absolute existence. He argues that it is not necessary for them to share in judgment, and he enumerates five types: (1) existential (wujūdī) like connective and social unity; (2) purely mental (iʿtibārī maḥḍ) which avoids infinite regress; (3) additional to the essence (zāʾid 'alā al-māhiyya) like the unity of man; (4) identical to the essence (nafs al-māhiyya) like the unity of unity; and (5) part of the essence (juzʾuhā). Additionally, they can be substance or accident. The commentary explains each point, noting that this flexibility prevents infinite regress and sophistry. The passage also remarks that the divisions of the real one (al-wāḥid al-ḥaqīqī) are the most deserving, except for the Necessary Being. The final commentary clarifies that the permissibility of substantiality in some cases does not contradict accidentalness in others.

Key Terms
وحدة
تشكيك
وجودي
اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All types of unity must share the same ontological judgment (e.g., all are existential).
Correction: The passage explicitly states that units of unity are different in reality due to equivocity, so they are not required to share the same judgment. Some may be existential, others mental, etc.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not necessary for all units of unity to share the same judgment?
A) Because unity is purely conceptual.
B) Because unity is diversified by equivocity (tashkīk), allowing different realities.
C) Because all units are existential.
D) Because unity is always additional to the essence.

Key Takeaway
Unity is not univocal; its types differ in reality while sharing the concept, so they may have different judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what ways do you currently conflate the unity of Allah with unity you experience in creation? How must your understanding of tawheed be purified by this lesson?
?
This lesson shows that sharing a concept does not imply sharing the same reality. Where in your own life have you mistakenly assumed that because two things share a label, they are essentially the same? How does this insight change your approach to distinguishing between Creator and creation?

The Five Types of Unity
Sanusi enumerates five types of unity and mentions substance/accident variation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate and distinguish the five types of unity: existential, purely mental, additional to essence, identical to essence, and part of essence, and understand their respective roles.

Core Idea
Unity comes in five varieties, each with a different ontological status.

Having established that unity units can differ in judgment, Sanusi now provides a detailed classification. He lists five types: existential (like connective unity), purely mental (to avoid regress), additional to essence (like man's unity), identical to essence (like unity of unity), and part of essence. He also notes that unity can be substance or accident in different cases.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration passage
Read the Arabic from 'فمنها ما هو وجودي' to the end of the passage. Let the list of five types be clear before analyzing each.
Notice the phrase 'فمنها' repeats for each type.
2
Identify each type and its definition
Match each 'منها' to the description: existential, purely mental, additional to essence, identical to essence, part of essence. Use the translation to clarify each.
The purely mental type is crucial: it stops infinite regress.
3
Understand the substance/accident point
Sanusi adds that unity can be a substance in some cases and an accident in others, reinforcing that judgments vary.
This is an example of 'سائر الأحكام'.
4
Reflect on the benefit
The passage ends with 'تنبه له' – be mindful of this principle, as it helps in many places.
This encourages applying the idea elsewhere.

When Are Two Things Truly 'Other'?
Sanusi contrasts two views on duality and otherness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two theological views on the relationship between duality and otherness, and why existence is a necessary condition for otherness.

Core Idea
Two things are not automatically 'other'—otherness requires existence and distinction.

This lesson examines a foundational logical and theological concept: what does it mean for two things to be 'other'? Sanusi presents the majority view that every two are others, and then the more refined position of our teachers, who argue that otherness is an attribute of existence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for discussions about the divine attributes and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes both the main text and commentary.
2
Identify the two views
Find the statement of the majority (al-jumhūr) and the statement of our teachers (mashāyikhnā). Note how they differ on whether every two are other.
The majority says 'every two are others'; teachers say 'not every two are others'.
3
Understand the condition of existence
The teachers require that the two things be existents (mawjūdān) that can separate in space or non-existence. Ask yourself why existence matters for otherness.
Otherness is a positive attribute, so non-existents cannot possess it.
4
Consider the exclusion of non-existents
The passage explains that non-existents are excluded because otherness is a positive attribute. Think about what this implies for comparing non-existent things to existent ones.
Also note the role of distinction (tamāyuz) – two others must be distinct.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this concept applies to theological discussions about the divine attributes and the relation between the Creator and creation.
This distinction prevents conflating mere multiplicity with genuine otherness.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد السابع : الاثنان هما الغيران

( الاثنان هما الغيران ( أي الاثنينية تستلزم التغاير هذا هو المشهور الذي ذهب إليه الجمهور، فكل اثنين عندهم غيران كما أن كل غيرين اثنين اتفاقاً . ( وقال مشايخنا : ( ليس كل اثنين بغيرين بل الغيران موجودان جاز انفكاكهما في حيز أو عدم فخرج بقيد الوجود (الأعدام) فإنها لا توصف بالتغاير عندهم؛ بناء على أن الغيرية الصفات الثبوتية فلا يتصف به عدمان، ولا عدم ووجود وهذا أعم من قوله : ( إذ لا تمايز فيها ، ولا بد في الغيرين من التمايز وذلك لاختصاصه بما يكون طرفاه عدميين .

قوله: (أي الاثنينية تستلزم التغاير أي في الوجود سواء كانتا متغايرتين بالذات أو بالاعتبار، فلا ينافي ما تقدم في مباحث الوجود من أن التغاير نفس الاثنينية، أو مستلزم لها، ففيه إشارة إلى أن قوله : الاثنان هما الغيران وإن أفاد حصر المسند إليه في المسند، أو العكس إلا أن المقصود هو الأول لأن الثاني لا نزاع فيه قوله : (الأعدام) أي المعدومات التي من جملتها الأعدام أيضاً، لأن خروج الأعدام إنما هو باعتبار أنها معدومة من حيث ذواتها فيشمل المعدومات كلها . قوله : ( فإنها لا توصف إلخ) دليل للإخراج المفهوم من الخروج، وقس عليه الدلائل الآتية أي إنما أخرجت لأنها ليست من أفراد المحدود. قوله : ( من الصفات الثبوتية ) أي الموجودة كالاختلاف والتضاد . قوله : ( وهذا أعم) أي ما ذكرنا من دليل عدم كونه من أفراد المحدود أعم مما ذكره المصنف؛ لإفادته عدم كون المعدوم والموجود أيضاً من أفراده، بخلاف ما ذكره المصنف قوله : ( ولا بد في الغيرين من التمايز) إذ لا بد فيهما من الاثنينية اتفاقاً، وهي لا تتحقق بدون التمايز. قوله : ( لاختصاصه) أي القول المذكور بما يكون أي لغيرين يكون طرفاه عدمين، أو قوله: (فإنها لا توصف بالتغاير عندهم هذا تعليل لإخراج المفهوم من الكلام، لا للخروج وأما علة الخروج عدم تحقق الوجود المأخوذ في التعريف بهما، وكذا الكلام في قول المصنف : إذ لا تمايز فيها كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( لاختصاصه بما يكون طرفاه عدميين) وذلك لأن الموجود ممتاز عن المعدوم
The Fourth Observatory - The Seventh Intention: The two (al-ithnān) are the other (al-ghīrān). (The two are the other) i.e., duality (al-ithnāniyya) entails otherness (al-taghāyur). This is the well-known (view) that the majority (al-jumhūr) held, so every two are for them other, just as every two others are two by agreement. (And our teachers (mashāyikhnā) said: Not every two are other, but the two others are existents (mawjūdān) permissible for their separation in space (ḥayyiz) or non‑existence, so by the condition of existence there are excluded (the non‑existents (al-a‘dām)), for they (the non‑existents) are not described by otherness according to them, based upon the fact that otherness (al-ghayriyya) is a positive attribute (al-ṣifāt al-thubūtiyya), so two non‑existents cannot be described by it, nor a non‑existent and an existent. And this is more general than his saying: (since there is no distinction (tamāyuz) in them), and there must be in the two others distinction, and that is because of its being restricted to what has its two sides non‑existent. His saying: (i.e., duality entails otherness) i.e., in existence, whether they are distinct by essence or by consideration, so it does not contradict what preceded in the discussions of existence that otherness is the same as duality, or entails it, for in it there is an indication that his saying: “The two are the other” although it gives the impression of restricting the predicate to the subject or the opposite, yet the intended is the first because there is no dispute about the second. His saying: (the non‑existents) i.e., the non‑existent things which include non‑existents as well, because the exclusion of non‑existents is only insofar as they are non‑existent in terms of their essences, so it includes all non‑existent things. His saying: (for they are not described etc.) is a proof for the exclusion understood from the word “excluded”, and analogize to it the following proofs, i.e., they were only excluded because they are not individuals of the defined term. His saying: (of the positive attributes) i.e., existing, like difference (al-ikhtilāf) and opposition (al-taḍād). His saying: (And this is more general) i.e., what we mentioned as a proof that it is not an individual of the defined term is more general than what the author mentioned, because it conveys that the non‑existent and the existent are also not among its individuals, unlike what the author mentioned. His saying: (And there must be in the two others distinction) because there must be duality in them by agreement, and it does not come about without distinction. His saying: (because of its being restricted) i.e., the mentioned saying to what is, i.e., for two others whose two sides are non‑existent, or his saying: (for they are not described by otherness according to them) this is a cause for the exclusion of the concept from the statement, not for the exclusion, but the cause of the exclusion is the non‑realization of the existence taken in the definition of the two, and likewise the statement in the author’s saying: “since there is no distinction in them”, as is not hidden. His saying: (because of its being restricted to what has its two sides non‑existent) and that is because the existent is distinguished from the non‑existent.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between duality (al-ithnāniyya) and otherness (al-taghāyur). It presents two views: (1) The majority (al-jumhūr) hold that every two are other (al-ithnān humā al-ghīrān), meaning that twoness entails otherness, and every pair of others are two by agreement. (2) Our teachers (mashāyikhnā) limit this: not every two are other; rather, two others must be existents that can separate in space or non‑existence. This excludes non‑existents (a‘dām) from being described as other, because otherness is a positive attribute (ṣifa thubūtiyya) that cannot apply to two non‑existents or a non‑existent and an existent. The commentary clarifies that distinction (tamāyuz) is necessary for otherness, and explains that the teachers’ condition is more general than the author’s phrase “since there is no distinction in them,” because it also excludes the case of one existent and one non‑existent. The commentary also discusses the relationship between otherness and duality, noting that otherness may be identical to duality or entailed by it, but the intended meaning of the phrase is the first.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any two things are automatically 'other' simply by being two.
Correction: The teachers show that otherness requires both existence and the possibility of separation. Non-existents or pairs of existent and non-existent do not qualify as 'other'.

Checkpoint
According to the view of 'our teachers' (mashāyikhnā), what condition prevents non-existents from being described as 'other'?
A) They lack existence.
B) They lack differentiation.
C) They are not two.
D) They are mental.

Key Takeaway
Otherness (taghāyur) is a positive attribute that requires existence and distinction; not every pair of two qualifies as 'other'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat non-existent scenarios or hypotheticals as 'other' to real things in your heart, as if they have the same weight? How does this lesson's condition—that otherness requires existence—change how you value the real over the imagined? Will you today consciously distinguish between what truly exists and what only seems to be?
?
This lesson says 'otherness is a positive attribute.' Which relationships or distinctions in your life are you treating as real 'others' when they might actually be only conceptual shadows? Commit to one relationship where you will honestly ask: 'Does this otherness actually exist, or is it merely assumed?'
?
The teachers taught that not every two are other. Where in your beliefs do you automatically assume separation or opposition between two things (e.g., your past and present, your faith and doubts) without checking if both truly exist and are distinct? What would it require you to do to align your inner distinctions with this truth?

Objection and Answer: Distinction Among Non-Existents
Objection and answer on distinction of non-existents, with two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the distinction among non-existents is only conceptual and not real, and why this is crucial for the theological doctrine of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Non-existents are distinct only in the mind, not in reality.

This passage addresses a key objection against the claim that non-existents cannot be distinct. The objector references an earlier statement that they are distinct, but the answer clarifies that such distinction is purely conceptual. Understanding this nuance is essential for grasping the theologians' position on divine attributes, which require real distinction among existents.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Start by reading the objection ('If you say...') and the immediate answer ('I say...'). Notice the structure: the objector appeals to an earlier claim, and the answer introduces the distinction between conceptual and real distinction.
The key phrase is 'according to their concepts, not according to what they are in themselves.'
2
Unpack the two interpretations of 'fata'dabbar'
The text then explains two possible meanings of 'their concepts' and 'what they apply to.' Identify both interpretations and see why one is false and the other is correct according to the commentator.
This is the core of the argument: universal vs. particular concepts, versus mental representation vs. reality.
3
Reflect on the condition for two things being 'different'
Note the condition: for two things to be different (ghayran), distinction must be in themselves, not just in the mind. This condition rules out real distinction among non-existents.
Relate this to the earlier discussion of taghayur.
4
Connect to divine attributes
The passage ends by hinting that the negation of real distinction among non-existents is necessary for the belief that Allah's attributes are multiple and existent in His essence. Consider how this fits into the broader theology.
This shows the practical importance of the abstract argument.

فإن قلت : أليس قد مر أن الأعدام متمايزة عند المتكلمين النافين للوجود الذهني؟ قلت : أليس أجيب عن ذلك بأن التمايز بينهما إنما هو بحسب مفهوماتها دون ما عليه، ولا بد في الغيرين من التمايز بحسب ما صدقا عليه، فتدبر . قوله : ( فتدبر ) حتى يظهر لك صحته وفساده فإنه إن أريد بمفهوماتها المعاني الكلية، وبما صدقت عليها أفرادها كان فاسداً، فإنه كما أن مفهوم عدم السواد متميز عن مفهوم عدم الضوء، كذلك فرده وهو عدم السواد المخصوص ممتاز عن عدم الضوء المخصوص مفهوماتها إذا كانت متميزة كيف تصدق على ما ليس بمتميز. وإن أريد بمفهوماتها ما حصل في العقل من حيث حصولها فيه، وبما صدقت هي عليه نفس تلك المعدومات مع قطع النظر عن الحصول العقلي، كان صحيحاً بلا شبهة لما مر من أن التمايز بينهما إنما هو في العقل إلا أن النافين للوجود الذهني لا يقولون : إن الحصول العقلي وجود ذهني بل هو تعلق بين العالم والمعلوم، ولا شك أن الغيرين لا بد من التمايز بينهما في أنفسهما مع قطع النظر عن الحصول العقلي، لأنهما من أقسام الموجودين في الخارج. واعلم أن ما ذكره الشارح إنما يظهر إذا جاز أن يقوم التمايز بشيء بالنسبة إلى آخر، من غير أن يقوم بذلك الآخر، وإلا فلا تمايز بين الموجود والمعدوم أيضاً؛ لأن المعدوم لا يتصف بالتمايز ، سواء قيس إلى موجود أو معدوم آخر؛ بناء على ما سبق من أن كل متميز فله وجود، إما في الذهن، أو في الخارج. والظاهر أن التميز يقوم بكل من المتميزين اللهم إلا أن يقال لو سلم عدم الامتياز بين الموجود والمعدوم أيضاً لم يقدح فيما ذكره لأن مراده أن قوله : لا تمايز في الأعدام حكم بعدم التمايز بينهما مختص بما يكون طرفاه عدميين وإن انتفى التمايز بين الموجود والمعدوم في نفس الأمر أيضاً، فيكون الدليل قاصراً عن المدعى حتى لو ضم إليه : ولا الموجود ولا في المعدوم ،لصح، وفيه تأمل. قوله : ( فتدبر) ليظهر لك فساده، فإنه كما أن مفهوم السواد يمتاز عن مفهوم عدم الضوء مثلاً، كذلك ذاته وهو عدم الضوء يمتاز عن عدم السواد مثلاً . وإن قلت : بالفرق فهو تحكم، كذا نقل عن الشارح، والحق أن القول بتمايز المعدومات بحسب ما صدقت هي عليه لا يلائم أصول المتكلمين كيف لا وقد صرح الشارح في بحث الموضوع أن انتفاء الحال وعدم تمايز المعدومات محتاج إليهما في اعتقاد كون صفاته تعالى متعددة موجودة في ذاته، وصرح
If you say: Did it not pass that non-existents are distinct (mutamayyizah) according to the theologians who negate mental existence? I say: Was it not answered that distinction (tamyiz) between them is only according to their concepts (mafhumathima) not according to what they are in themselves, and in the two different there must be distinction according to what they actually apply to (ma sadaqa 'alayh)? So reflect. His statement: 'So reflect' so that its correctness and falsity become clear to you. For if by 'their concepts' is meant the universal meanings, and by 'what they apply to' their individuals, then it is false: for just as the concept of absence of blackness is distinct from the concept of absence of light, similarly its individual, i.e., the specific absence of blackness, is distinct from the specific absence of light. If their concepts are distinct, how can they apply to what is not distinct? And if by 'their concepts' is meant what is obtained in the mind in terms of its occurrence there, and by 'what they apply to' is meant those very non-existents apart from mental consideration, then it is correct without doubt, because it has passed that distinction between them is only in the mind, except that those who negate mental existence do not say that mental obtaining is mental existence; rather it is a relation between the knower and the known. And there is no doubt that the two different must have distinction between themselves in themselves apart from mental consideration, because they are among the types of existents in the external world. And know that what the commentator mentioned appears only if it is permissible that distinction (tamyiz) inheres in a thing relative to another without inhering in that other; otherwise, there is no distinction between the existent and the non-existent either, because the non-existent cannot be described with distinction, whether compared to an existent or another non-existent, based on what preceded: that every distinct thing has existence, either in the mind or in the external. And it is apparent that distinction inheres in each of the two distinct things, unless it is said: If non-distinction between the existent and the non-existent is also conceded, it does not harm what he mentioned, because his intent is that his statement 'no distinction among non-existents' is a judgment of non-distinction between them, specific to what has both extremes non-existent; even if distinction between the existent and the non-existent is also negated in reality, then the proof would be insufficient for the claim, so that if he added: 'Nor the existent nor the non-existent,' it would be correct. And in it there is consideration. His statement: 'So reflect' so that its falsity becomes clear to you, for just as the concept of blackness is distinct from the concept of absence of light, for example, so too its essence, i.e., absence of light, is distinct from absence of blackness, for example. And if you say: there is a difference, it is arbitrary. Thus it is transmitted from the commentator. And the truth is that the statement that non-existents are distinct according to what they actually apply to does not conform to the principles of the theologians. How not, when the commentator declared in the discussion of the subject that the negation of state (al-hal) and the non-distinction of non-existents are both needed in the belief that His attributes, may He be exalted, are multiple and existent in His essence, and he declared
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion concerning whether non-existents (al-a'dam) can be distinguished (tamyiz) from each other. The objector points out that earlier it was stated that non-existents are distinct according to the theologians who deny mental existence. The answer is that the distinction there is only in terms of their concepts (mafahim), not in terms of what they are in themselves (ma 'alayh). For two things to be 'different' (ghayran), distinction must be in the things themselves, not just in the mind. The commentary then unpacks the phrase 'fata'dabbar' (so reflect), presenting two possible interpretations of 'mafhumathima' and 'ma sadaqat 'alayh'. If the first means universal meanings and the second means individuals, the argument is flawed because the individual non-existents would still be distinct. But if the first means mental representations and the second means the non-existents themselves apart from mental consideration, then the argument is correct: distinction of non-existents is only mental, and they lack real distinction in themselves. The commentator also discusses whether tamyiz inheres in both parties or can be relative, and notes that the negation of tamyiz among non-existents is required for the theological doctrine of divine attributes. The passage ends abruptly with 'wa sarraha' (and he declared), indicating that the argument continues beyond this extract.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Someone might think that if two non-existents have distinct concepts, then they are distinct in reality.
Correction: The passage clarifies that conceptual distinction does not entail real distinction; real distinction requires the things themselves to be distinct apart from mental consideration.

Checkpoint
What is the key condition required for two things to be considered 'different' (ghayran) according to the passage?
A) They must have distinct concepts
B) They must have distinction in themselves (fi anfusihima) apart from mental consideration
C) They must be both existent in the external world
D) They must be both non-existent

Key Takeaway
Non-existents lack real distinction; their distinction is only in the mind, which is essential for the theological position that Allah's attributes are multiple and existent in His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this truth affect the way you approach His names and attributes in your prayers? Will you consciously affirm today that His attributes are all one in His essence?
?
The passage shows that even non-existents have conceptual distinction but no real difference. This discussion presupposes that for two things to be truly different, they must be distinct in themselves. In your own life, do you differentiate things based on their true nature or merely on mental concepts? Examine one relationship or conflict where you might be treating conceptual differences as real, and commit to seeing it through the lens of truth.

The Distinction of Non-Existents: An Objection Answered
Objection and answer regarding distinction of non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the theologians respond to the objection that non-existents are distinct and why their distinction is only conceptual, not real.

Core Idea
Non-existents are distinct only in concept, not in reality.

Theologians who deny mental existence face a puzzle: if non-existents have no reality, how can they be distinct? This passage presents an objection and a nuanced answer that distinguishes between conceptual and real distinction. The author guides us through the logic and its implications for the principles of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
Read the opening objection: 'Did it not pass that non-existents are distinct according to the theologians who deny mental existence?' Identify the tension this creates.
The objector is pointing to a prior statement that seems to contradict the claim that non-existents have no real distinction.
2
Analyze the Initial Answer
The response begins by distinguishing between distinction by concepts (mafhumāt) and distinction in reality. Grasp why this distinction is made and what it achieves.
This is a key move: conceptual distinction does not imply real distinction.
3
Examine the Deeper Clarification
The passage then examines two possible interpretations of 'concepts' and 'what they are true of', showing the implications. Focus on how the second interpretation preserves the kalam position.
This part involves subtle reasoning about mental existence and the nature of distinction.

عليه، فتدبر. وذلك لأن الدليل المذكور سابقاً وهو أنها أي المعدومات نفي صرف لا إشارة إليها أصلاً، إنما ينتهض على عدم تمايزها، لا على عدم تمايز المعدوم والموجود؛ لأن الموجود ليس نفياً صرفاً. وما قيل : إن التمايز بشيء بالنسبة إلى آخر، من غير أن يقوم بذلك الآخر، وإلا فلا تمايز بين الموجود والمعدوم أيضاً؛ لأن المعدوم لا يتصف بالتمايز، سواء قيس إلى موجود أو معدوم آخر؛ بناء على ما سبق من أن كل متميز فله وجود، إما في الذهن، أو في الخارج. والظاهر أن التميز يقوم بكل من المتميزين اللهم إلا أن يقال لو سلم عدم الامتياز بين الموجود والمعدوم أيضاً لم يقدح فيما ذكره لأن مراده أن قوله : لا تمايز في الأعدام حكم بعدم التمايز بينهما مختص بما يكون طرفاه عدميين، وإن انتفى التمايز بين الموجود والمعدوم في نفس الأمر أيضاً، فيكون الدليل قاصراً عن المدعى حتى لو ضم إليه : ولا الموجود ولا في المعدوم، لصح، وفيه تأمل. في قوله : ( فتدبر) ليظهر لك فساده، فإنه كما أن مفهوم السواد يمتاز عن مفهوم عدم الضوء مثلاً، كذلك ذاته وهو عدم الضوء يمتاز عن عدم السواد مثلاً. وإن قلت : بالفرق فهو تحكم، كذا نقل عن الشارح، والحق أن القول بتمايز المعدومات بحسب ما صدقت هي عليه لا يلائم أصول المتكلمين كيف لا وقد صرح الشارح في بحث الموضوع أن انتفاء الحال وعدم تمايز المعدومات محتاج إليهما في اعتقاد كون صفاته تعالى متعددة موجودة في ذاته،
