Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sharing any attribute would make things similar, violating the definition of mutakhalifan.
Correction: The definition only negates sharing in all essential attributes; sharing some essential attributes does not make things identical.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT an essential attribute (sifa nafsiyya)?
A) Existence (al-wujud)
B) Subsistence in a subject (al-qiyam bil-mahal)
C) Temporal origination (al-huduth)
D) Accidentality (al-'aradiyya)

Key Takeaway
Mutakhalifan may share essential attributes like existence and subsistence without becoming similar; only identity requires sharing all essential attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You define yourself by many attributes—believer, student, friend. Which of these are truly essential to who you are? Do you sometimes confuse temporary or accidental attributes with your core identity? This lesson teaches precision in definition. How might that precision help you clarify your purpose and relationship with Allah?
?
When you judge others, do you focus on shared attributes that are superficial or deeper essential ones? This lesson warns against assuming that sharing any attribute makes two things the same. Ask yourself: Have you wrongly categorized someone because of a shared superficial trait? What would it take to see their true essence as a unique creation of Allah?
?
The passage ends with 'fa-tadabbar' (so ponder). Take a moment to ponder your own existence: you share existence with everything, yet you are distinct. How does this realization humble you and increase your gratitude for the specific way Allah has crafted you—not as a generic being, but as a unique individual with a specific role?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
6 min
Do Different Things Sharing an Attribute Count as Similar?
Al-Jurjani discusses terminological dispute on similarity in shared attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the dispute over calling two different things 'similar' in a shared attribute is terminological, and that applying such terms to God depends on revealed law (tawqif).

Core Idea
Likeness in meaning may exist, but naming it is a matter of terminology and revelation.

In this passage, al-Jurjani tackles a nuanced question: can two essentially different things that share a single attribute be called 'similar' in that respect? The answer, he explains, hinges on distinguishing between real likeness in meaning and the verbal application of the term. We'll examine the positions of key Ash'ari scholars and al-Najjar, and see how the principle of tawqif (reliance on revelation) governs divine names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for overall sense, then read the translation. Notice how the author frames the question as a matter of terminology.
The passage includes parenthetical explanations that are part of the original commentary.
2
Identify the core question and the reduction to terminology
The passage begins with whether mutakhalifan (different things) sharing an attribute can be called 'two similars'. It then states that the dispute reduces to mere terminology (yari'u ila mujarrad al-istilah).
This is the key move: the reality of likeness is not denied, only the name.
3
Examine the Ash'ari position and al-Najjar's stance
Al-Qadi and al-Qalanisi allow the term in temporal things. Al-Najjar defines similarity as sharing a positive attribute, leading to God being similar to creation in existence (in meaning).
Notice that al-Najjar accepts likeness in meaning but may reject the verbal application to God.
4
Understand the role of revelation (tawqif)
The passage explains that the basis for applying the word 'similar' to God is the Revealed Law, according to those who hold God's names to be tawqifiyya (derived from revelation).
Thus, al-Najjar can commit to similarity in meaning while refraining from the verbal application due to lack of scriptural warrant.
5
Grasp the final nuance: restricted similarity
The passage clarifies that 'two similars' here means restricted to that shared attribute, not absolute. Also, it may be permissible to say the created is similar to the Creator but not vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل يسميان ) أي هل يسمى المتخالفان المتشاركان في بعض الصفات النفسية أو غيرها (مثلين باعتبار ما اشتركا فيه من الصفة النفسية أو غيرها، لهم فيه ( تردد) وخلاف، (ويرجع إلى مجرد الاصطلاح؛ لأن المماثلة في ذلك المشترك ثابتة بحسب المعنى، والمنازعة في إطلاق الاسم. قال القاضي والقلانسي من الأشاعرة : لا مانع من ذلك في الحوادث معنى ولفظاً، إذا لم يرد التماثل في غير ما وقع فيه الاشتراك، حتى صرح القلانسي بأن كل مشتركين في الحدوث متماثلان فيه : أي في الحدوث. ( وعليه ) أي على ما ذكر من إطلاق المتماثلين على المتخالفين باعتبار ما اشتركا فيه ( يحمل قول النجار في تعريف التماثل بالاشتراك في صفة إثبات، ( فالله مماثل عنده للحوادث في وجوده عقلاً) أي بحسب المعنى. ( والنزاع في الإطلاق) أي إطلاق لفظ المماثل للحوادث عليه تعالى (ومأخذه ) أي مأخذ الإطلاق (السمع)، عند من يجعل أسماء الله تعالى توقيفية، فللنجار أن يلتزم التماثل بين الرب والمربوب معنى وإن منع إطلاق اللفظ عليه. وأما الاعتراض عليه بتماثل السواد والبياض، فهو كما مر مدفوع عنه بالالتزام معنى ولفظاً. قوله : (مثلين) أي مقيدين بتلك الصفة لا مطلقاً، فإنهما المتشاركان في جميع تلك الصفات. قوله : ( وإن منع إطلاق اللفظ (عليه قيل وعلى هذا ينبغي جواز أن يقال : المربوب مماثل للرب، وإن لم يجز الرب مماثل للمربوب ؛ إذ ذلك الإطلاق لا يستلزم هذا الإطلاق .
And do they call them? That is, are the two different things that share in some essential or other attributes called 'two similars' by virtue of what they share of the essential or other attribute? Concerning this there is hesitation and disagreement. It reduces to mere terminology, because likeness (al-mumathala) in that shared [attribute] is established in meaning, while the dispute is about applying the name. Al-Qadi and al-Qalanisi from the Ash'aris said: There is no objection to that in temporal things in meaning and wording, if similarity is not intended in something other than what they share. So much so that al-Qalanisi stated that any two things sharing in temporal origination are identical in it, i.e., in temporal origination. And upon this (i.e., upon what was mentioned of applying the term 'two similars' to the two different in virtue of what they share) is based the saying of al-Najjar in his definition of similarity as sharing in a positive attribute. So God, according to him, is similar to temporal things in His existence rationally, i.e., in meaning. And the dispute is about the application (i.e., applying the word 'similar' to temporal things to Him, Exalted). And its basis is the Revealed Law (al-sam'), according to those who consider God's names to be discretionary (tawqifiyya). So al-Najjar may commit to similarity between the Lord and the servant in meaning, even if he forbids applying the word to Him. As for the objection against him based on the similarity of black and white, it is, as previously, repelled from him by committing to it in meaning and wording. His statement: 'two similars' means restricted by that attribute, not absolutely, for they are the two that share in all those attributes. His statement: 'And even if he forbids applying the word to Him,' it is said: upon this, it should be permissible to say that the servant is similar to the Lord, even though it is not permissible that the Lord is similar to the servant, for that application does not entail this application.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage addresses the question: Can two things that are different (mutakhalifan) but share some attribute be called 'similar' in that respect? The author states there is disagreement, but it is purely terminological: the likeness in the shared attribute is real in meaning, but the verbal application is disputed. The Qadi and al-Qalanisi from the Ash'aris allow it in temporal things both in meaning and wording, provided similarity is not claimed in other respects. Al-Qalanisi even says that all things sharing huduth (temporal origination) are similar in that respect. Upon this principle, al-Najjar's definition of similarity as sharing in a positive attribute is interpreted: God is similar to temporal things in existence rationally (in meaning). The dispute then is about whether to apply the word 'similar' to God, with the basis being the Revealed Law (al-sam'), since some hold that God's names are derived from revelation (tawqif). So al-Najjar can accept similarity in meaning but not in wording. The objection based on the similarity of black and white is dismissed by accepting it in both meaning and wording. Finally, clarifications are given: when they say 'two similars' in this context, it means restricted to that attribute, not absolute similarity. Also, even if one refrains from applying 'similar' to God, it may be permissible to say the created is similar to the Creator, since that does not imply the converse.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two things that share an attribute are said to be absolutely similar in every respect.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'two similars' in this context is restricted to the shared attribute only, not absolute similarity.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the dispute about applying the word 'similar' to God according to this passage?
A) Reason
B) The Revealed Law (al-sam')
C) The consensus of scholars
D) The language

Key Takeaway
The dispute over calling different things 'similar' in a shared attribute is terminological, and applying such terms to God ultimately rests on revelation, not reason alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God shares existence with you as a real attribute, do you ever treat that likeness as a license for familiarity or irreverence? How can you ensure your awareness of that shared attribute leads to gratitude, not presumption?
?
The dispute about calling God 'similar' to creation is settled by revelation. Is there any attribute of God that you have described without scriptural warrant? What would it mean to submit even your theological language to Allah's own words?
?
Al-Najjar acknowledged similarity in meaning but refrained from using the word. Are there truths about your relationship with Allah that you acknowledge but refuse to verbalize because they feel too intimate or risky? What is holding you back?

Similarity and the Role of Convention
Defines similarity and clarifies the conventional nature of the term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that similarity (tamāthul) between two different things sharing an attribute is a matter of convention, not a rational necessity.

Core Idea
Similarity is about shared attributes, but calling something 'similar' is a linguistic choice.

Before diving into theological disputes, al-Isfahani clarifies the conceptual framework. He distinguishes between types of attributes and establishes that similarity in a shared attribute is real in meaning, yet the application of the term 'similar' depends on convention. This lesson unpacks that foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow. Then read the translation to understand the meaning.
Focus on the distinction between attributes of the self and meaningful attributes.
2
Identify the key concepts
Find the definitions of 'similarity' and the two types of attributes (nafsiyya and maʿnawiyya). Notice how the author presents the dispute.
The dispute is about applying the term, not about the reality of the shared attribute.
3
Understand the conventional nature
The passage states that the disagreement reduces to mere convention (iṣṭilāḥ). Reflect on why calling two things 'similar' is not a purely rational issue.
This connects to the broader theme of language and revelation in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد التاسع : الاثنان من المتكلمين ثلاثة أقسام
أيضاً من صفات النفس، بخلاف الحدوث والتحيز، فإنهما من الصفات المعنوية كما مر . ( وهل يسميان ) أي هل يسمى المتخالفان المتشاركان في بعض الصفات النفسية أو غيرها (مثلين باعتبار ما اشتركا فيه من الصفة النفسية أو غيرها، لهم فيه ( تردد) وخلاف، (ويرجع إلى مجرد الاصطلاح؛ لأن المماثلة في ذلك المشترك ثابتة بحسب المعنى، والمنازعة في إطلاق الاسم. قال القاضي والقلانسي من الأشاعرة : لا مانع من ذلك في الحوادث معنى ولفظاً، إذا لم يرد التماثل في غير ما وقع فيه الاشتراك، حتى صرح القلانسي بأن كل مشتركين في الحدوث متماثلان فيه : أي في الحدوث. ( وعليه ) أي على ما ذكر من إطلاق المتماثلين على المتخالفين باعتبار ما اشتركا فيه ( يحمل قول النجار في تعريف التماثل بالاشتراك في صفة إثبات، ( فالله مماثل عنده للحوادث في وجوده عقلاً) أي بحسب المعنى. ( والنزاع في الإطلاق) أي إطلاق لفظ المماثل للحوادث عليه تعالى (ومأخذه ) أي مأخذ الإطلاق (السمع)، عند من يجعل أسماء الله تعالى توقيفية، فللنجار أن يلتزم التماثل بين الرب والمربوب معنى وإن منع إطلاق اللفظ عليه. وأما الاعتراض عليه بتماثل السواد والبياض، فهو كما مر مدفوع عنه بالالتزام معنى ولفظاً.
The Fourth Observatory - The Ninth Objective: The two [things] according to the mutakallimun are three divisions. Also from the attributes of the self, unlike occurrence (al-ḥudūth) and occupying space (al-taḥayyuz), for they are among the meaningful attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) as previously mentioned. (And are they called) i.e., are the two different things (al-mutakhālifān) that share in some attributes of the self or others called (similar (mithlayn) by virtue of what they share of the attribute of the self or others? There is hesitation and disagreement concerning it, and it returns to mere convention; because similarity in that shared [attribute] is established according to meaning, and the dispute is over the application of the term. Al-Qāḍī and al-Qalānisī of the Ashʿarīs said: There is no obstacle to that in originated things (al-ḥawādith) in both meaning and wording, if similarity is not intended in other than what the sharing occurs in, such that al-Qalānisī declared that every two things sharing in occurrence (al-ḥudūth) are similar in it, i.e., in occurrence. (And upon it) i.e., upon what was mentioned of applying the term 'similar' to differing things by virtue of what they share, is carried al-Najjār's statement in his definition of similarity as sharing in a positive attribute, so God is similar in his view to originated things in His existence intellectually (ʿaqlan), i.e., according to meaning. (And the dispute is over the application) i.e., applying the word 'similar' to originated things to Him, the Exalted, (and its basis) i.e., the basis of application is revelation (al-samʿ), according to those who consider God's names to be tawqīfī (only revealed). So al-Najjār can affirm similarity between the Lord and the created in meaning, even if he prevents the application of the word to Him. As for the objection against him by the similarity of blackness and whiteness, it is, as previously mentioned, repelled by affirming both meaning and wording.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of similarity (al-tamāthul) in the context of the mutakallimūn's classification of the two things into three divisions (as per the earlier framework). It begins by distinguishing between attributes of the self (ṣifāt al-nafs) and meaningful attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya), noting that occurrence and spatial occupation are meaningful attributes. The core discussion is whether two things that differ in essence but share some attribute can be called 'similar' by virtue of that shared attribute. The author states that this is a matter of convention (istilāḥ) because the similarity in the shared attribute is established in meaning, but the dispute is over applying the term. He then presents the view of al-Qāḍī and al-Qalānisī who permit calling originated things similar in both meaning and wording if the similarity is restricted to the shared attribute. Al-Qalānisī even explicitly says that anything sharing occurrence is similar in that respect. Next, he explains that al-Najjār's definition of similarity as 'sharing in a positive attribute' leads to the conclusion that God is similar to created things in existence intellectually, but the dispute is only over applying the word 'similar' to God. The basis for this word application is revelation (al-samʿ) according to those who hold that God's names are derived only from revelation (tawqīf). Thus, al-Najjār can accept similarity in meaning but not in verbal usage. The passage ends by addressing an objection comparing this to the similarity of blackness and whiteness, which is dismissed by affirming both meaning and wording in that case.

الاصطلاح (al-iṣṭilāḥ)

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the dispute over calling two different things 'similar' considered a matter of convention?
A) Because the meaning of similarity is not established.
B) Because the similarity in the shared attribute is real but the application of the term is disputed.
C) Because the two things are actually identical.
D) Because the mutakallimun do not agree on the definition of similarity.

Key Takeaway
Similarity in a shared attribute is real in meaning, but calling two things 'similar' is a linguistic convention, not a rational necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a label you have used to describe someone or something as 'similar' to another. Was that label based on genuine similarity in one attribute, or did you assume it applied in all respects? How might the lesson's emphasis on convention make you more careful about such labels?
?
When have you insisted on a theological term or classification because it felt 'right' to you, without realizing it was a matter of convention? How can you distinguish between objective meaning and conventional labeling in your own faith discussions?
?
The passage shows that even scholars disagreed on applying the term 'similar'. Are there labels in your own life—about yourself, others, or even about God—that you hold rigidly but might be more flexible than you think? What would it cost you to loosen your grip on that label?

Can God Be Called Similar to Creatures?
Discusses al-Najjār's view on God's similarity to creatures and the role of revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute between al-Najjār and other scholars over whether God can be called 'similar' to creatures, and how revelation (samʿ) governs the application of divine names.

Core Idea
Al-Najjār accepts similarity in meaning but not in wording, while others reject both.

The previous lesson established that similarity in a shared attribute is real in meaning, but the application of the term is conventional. Now, al-Isfahani applies this to the most sensitive case: the relationship between God and His creatures. The key figure is al-Najjār, who defined similarity as sharing in a positive attribute, leading to God being intellectually similar to creatures in existence. This lesson examines his view and the objection against it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text for this lesson. It builds on the previous discussion and focuses on al-Najjār's view.
Notice the phrase 'فالله مماثل عنده للحوادث في وجوده عقلاً'.
2
Analyze al-Najjār's position
Al-Najjār defines similarity as sharing in a positive attribute. He concludes that God is similar to originated things in existence (intellectually). But he does not necessarily apply the word 'similar' to God. Why?
The basis for word application is revelation (samʿ). This is important for those who hold that God's names are tawqīfiyya.
3
Understand the objection
The passage ends with an objection comparing this to the similarity of blackness and whiteness. How is it refuted?
The refutation is by affirming both meaning and wording in that case, but for God the wording is restricted by revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد التاسع : الاثنان من المتكلمين ثلاثة أقسام
أيضاً من صفات النفس، بخلاف الحدوث والتحيز، فإنهما من الصفات المعنوية كما مر . ( وهل يسميان ) أي هل يسمى المتخالفان المتشاركان في بعض الصفات النفسية أو غيرها (مثلين باعتبار ما اشتركا فيه من الصفة النفسية أو غيرها، لهم فيه ( تردد) وخلاف، (ويرجع إلى مجرد الاصطلاح؛ لأن المماثلة في ذلك المشترك ثابتة بحسب المعنى، والمنازعة في إطلاق الاسم. قال القاضي والقلانسي من الأشاعرة : لا مانع من ذلك في الحوادث معنى ولفظاً، إذا لم يرد التماثل في غير ما وقع فيه الاشتراك، حتى صرح القلانسي بأن كل مشتركين في الحدوث متماثلان فيه : أي في الحدوث. ( وعليه ) أي على ما ذكر من إطلاق المتماثلين على المتخالفين باعتبار ما اشتركا فيه ( يحمل قول النجار في تعريف التماثل بالاشتراك في صفة إثبات، ( فالله مماثل عنده للحوادث في وجوده عقلاً) أي بحسب المعنى. ( والنزاع في الإطلاق) أي إطلاق لفظ المماثل للحوادث عليه تعالى (ومأخذه ) أي مأخذ الإطلاق (السمع)، عند من يجعل أسماء الله تعالى توقيفية، فللنجار أن يلتزم التماثل بين الرب والمربوب معنى وإن منع إطلاق اللفظ عليه. وأما الاعتراض عليه بتماثل السواد والبياض، فهو كما مر مدفوع عنه بالالتزام معنى ولفظاً.
The Fourth Observatory - The Ninth Objective: The two [things] according to the mutakallimun are three divisions. Also from the attributes of the self, unlike occurrence (al-ḥudūth) and occupying space (al-taḥayyuz), for they are among the meaningful attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) as previously mentioned. (And are they called) i.e., are the two different things (al-mutakhālifān) that share in some attributes of the self or others called (similar (mithlayn) by virtue of what they share of the attribute of the self or others? There is hesitation and disagreement concerning it, and it returns to mere convention; because similarity in that shared [attribute] is established according to meaning, and the dispute is over the application of the term. Al-Qāḍī and al-Qalānisī of the Ashʿarīs said: There is no obstacle to that in originated things (al-ḥawādith) in both meaning and wording, if similarity is not intended in other than what the sharing occurs in, such that al-Qalānisī declared that every two things sharing in occurrence (al-ḥudūth) are similar in it, i.e., in occurrence. (And upon it) i.e., upon what was mentioned of applying the term 'similar' to differing things by virtue of what they share, is carried al-Najjār's statement in his definition of similarity as sharing in a positive attribute, so God is similar in his view to originated things in His existence intellectually (ʿaqlan), i.e., according to meaning. (And the dispute is over the application) i.e., applying the word 'similar' to originated things to Him, the Exalted, (and its basis) i.e., the basis of application is revelation (al-samʿ), according to those who consider God's names to be tawqīfī (only revealed). So al-Najjār can affirm similarity between the Lord and the created in meaning, even if he prevents the application of the word to Him. As for the objection against him by the similarity of blackness and whiteness, it is, as previously mentioned, repelled by affirming both meaning and wording.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of similarity (al-tamāthul) in the context of the mutakallimūn's classification of the two things into three divisions (as per the earlier framework). It begins by distinguishing between attributes of the self (ṣifāt al-nafs) and meaningful attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya), noting that occurrence and spatial occupation are meaningful attributes. The core discussion is whether two things that differ in essence but share some attribute can be called 'similar' by virtue of that shared attribute. The author states that this is a matter of convention (istilāḥ) because the similarity in the shared attribute is established in meaning, but the dispute is over applying the term. He then presents the view of al-Qāḍī and al-Qalānisī who permit calling originated things similar in both meaning and wording if the similarity is restricted to the shared attribute. Al-Qalānisī even explicitly says that anything sharing occurrence is similar in that respect. Next, he explains that al-Najjār's definition of similarity as 'sharing in a positive attribute' leads to the conclusion that God is similar to created things in existence intellectually, but the dispute is only over applying the word 'similar' to God. The basis for this word application is revelation (al-samʿ) according to those who hold that God's names are derived only from revelation (tawqīf). Thus, al-Najjār can accept similarity in meaning but not in verbal usage. The passage ends by addressing an objection comparing this to the similarity of blackness and whiteness, which is dismissed by affirming both meaning and wording in that case.

Key Terms
النجار (al-Najjār)
توقيفية (tawqīfiyya)
السمع (al-samʿ)
مماثل (mumāthil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Najjār's claim that God is similar to creatures implies anthropomorphism (tashbīh).
Correction: Al-Najjār only affirms similarity in existence intellectually (meaning), not in wording, and he does not apply the term 'similar' to God verbally due to revelation-based restrictions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for applying the term 'similar' to God according to those who hold God's names are tawqīfiyya?
A) Reason alone
B) Convention
C) Revelation (al-samʿ)
D) Consensus

Key Takeaway
Al-Najjār affirms God's similarity to creatures in meaning (intellectually) but restricts verbal application due to the tawqīfī principle that divine names require revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God in your own words, do you ever catch yourself using terms that imply similarity to created things? How does al-Najjār's insistence on restricting verbal application to revelation challenge you to be more careful with your speech about God? Will you commit to checking whether a divine name or attribute is revealed before using it?
?
The passage distinguishes between accepting similarity in meaning but refusing to say it aloud. Is there an area of your faith where you intellectually believe something but are hesitant to profess it publicly? What is that area, and what would it take for you to align your speech with your belief—or your belief with revelation?
?
Al-Najjār's position was attacked with the example of blackness and whiteness. Think of a time when someone criticized your faith using a false analogy. Did you dismiss it as easily as the text dismisses this objection? If not, why? Are you prepared to defend the unique nature of God against unwarranted comparisons?

Two Similars Cannot Coexist: The Ash'ari Position
Al-Isfahani states the Ash'ari principle that two similars cannot coexist and refutes the claim that they fall under the definition of mutadadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari principle that two similars cannot coexist and why they are not considered a subset of opposites.

Core Idea
Two similars cannot coexist, but not because they are opposites.

This passage addresses a subtle but important point in kalam: the relationship between similars and opposites. Al-Isfahani presents the Ash'ari view that two similars cannot coexist, but then refutes the potential misunderstanding that this makes them a type of opposite. Understanding this distinction clarifies the logic behind the classification of pairs in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for an overview. Then read the literal translation. Focus on the main claim and the objection that follows.
The passage is dense with commentary. Notice the dialogue structure between the author and an imaginary objector.
2
Identify the principle and the misconception
State the principle: Every two similars cannot coexist. Then identify the misconception: one might think this makes them a subcategory of opposites (mutadadd). Find where the text raises this objection.
3
Understand the refutation
The author gives two reasons why similars are not opposites. First, the reason for impossibility of coexistence is different. Second, similars can be substances, while opposites are defined as two meanings (accidents). Trace these arguments in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد العاشر : كل متماثلين لا يجتمعان
في التماثل أيضاً، فلا يكون هذا الخلاف مبنياً على الخلاف في الغيرين.
[
المقصد العاشر : كل متماثلين لا يجتمعان ]
أنه يجب
كل متماثلين فإنهما لا يجتمعان وإليه ذهب (الشيخ الأشعري، وقد يتوهم عليه أن يجعلهما قسماً من المتضادين لدخولهما في حدهما. وحينئذ ينقسم الاثنان قسمة ثنائية إلى المتخالفين والمتضادين، كما انقسما على رأى بعضهم إلى المتماثلين والمتخالفين على ما عرفت . والحق أنه لا وجوب عليه ولا دخول لهما في حد المتضادين، أما الأول فلان امتناع اجتماعهما عنده ليس لتضادهما على ما توهم، بل لما سيأتي . وأما الثاني فلأن المثلين قد يكونان جوهرين، فلا يندرجان تحت معنيين، فإن قلت : إذا كانا معنيين كسوادين مثلاً كانا مندرجين في الحد قطعاً
قوله : ( كل متماثلين فإنهما لا يجتمعان إما لانتفاء المحل كما في الجوهرين، أو لانتفاء الاجتماع فيه كما في العرضين ولذا لم يقل في محل ،واحد ومن زاد هذا القيد خص المتماثلين بالعرضين كما في شرح المقاصد قوله : (قسمة ثنائية إلخ ) بأن يقال : الاثنان إن امتنع اجتماعهما فهما متضادان، وإلا فهما متخالفان، وينقسم المتخالفان إلى المتماثلين وغيرهما .
قوله : ( لا وجوب عليه سواء كانا داخلين في حد المتضادين أولا . قوله: (ليس لتضادهما ) أي لتخالفهما في المتضادين بل للزوم الاتحاد، ورفع الاثنينية مما سيجيء، فهما نوعان متباينان وإن اشتركا في امتناع الاجتماع. قوله : ( وأما الثاني) أي عدم الدخول في الحد ، سواء كان الدخول موجباً لجعلهما قسماً المتضادين أو لا، إذ لو خص بالموجب لجعلهما قسما من المتضادين لم يرد الاعتراض بقوله : فإن قلت إلخ، كما لا يخفى .
من
قوله : (كانا مندرجين في الحد قطعاً فلا يصح جعل المتماثلين مطلقاً قسماً للمتضادين، فهذا اعتراض منشؤه قوله : فلا يندرجان تحت معنيين، وليس إثباتاً للمقدمة أعني دخولهما في الحد .
قوله : فلا يكون هذا الخلاف مبنياً إلخ قيل : تفصيل المبحث أن منهم من لم يشترط التغاير في التماثل والاختلاف ومنهم القاضي، ومنهم من اشترط والمشترطون إن قالوا : بالتغاير بالصفات قالوا بالوصف بالتماثل والاختلاف فيها أيضاً، وإن لم يقولوا به لم يقولوا بهما أيضاً. فمراد المصنف بقوله : عائد إشارة إلى التفصيل على تقدير شرط ،التغاير لا أن الوصف بالتغاير شرط البتة فالمراد بقوله : ومنهم من يصفها بهما هو الجمهور لا القاضي، حتى يرد ما ذكره الشارح . وهذا القول ليس ببعيد إلا أن الآمدي لم يذكر قول البعض بالتمثيل والاختلاف بناء على القول بالتغاير، والله أعلم. قوله : ( وإليه ذهب الشيخ الأشعري سيجيء في المقصد الثاني من موقف الإلهيات أن
The Fourth Observatory - The Tenth Objective: Every two similars do not coexist. Also in similarity, so this disagreement is not built on the disagreement about the two others. [The Tenth Objective: Every two similars do not coexist] That it is necessary: Every two similars, they do not coexist, and to this went (Shaykh al-Ashʿarī). And it may be mistakenly thought that he makes them a division of the opposites (al-mutaḍāddīn) because they enter under their definition. Then the two are divided by a binary division into the different (al-mutakhālifayn) and the opposite (al-mutaḍāddīn), just as they were divided according to some into the similar and the different, as you know. The truth is that there is no obligation upon him, nor do they enter into the definition of opposites. As for the first, because the impossibility of their coexistence according to him is not due to their being opposite as mistakenly thought, but for what will come. As for the second, because the two similars may be two substances, so they are not subsumed under two meanings [i.e., two accidents]. If you say: If they are two meanings like two blacknesses, then they would be included in the definition absolutely. His statement: (Every two similars, they do not coexist, either due to the absence of a locus as in two substances, or due to the absence of coexistence in it as in two accidents, and therefore he did not say 'in one locus'. And whoever added this restriction restricted the similars to accidents, as in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid. His statement: (binary division etc.) by saying: The two, if their coexistence is impossible, they are opposites, otherwise they are different, and the different divide into the similar and other than them. His statement: (No obligation upon him) whether they are inside the definition of opposites or not. His statement: (Not due to their being opposite) i.e., due to their being different in opposites, but due to the necessity of unity and the removal of twoness, which will come. So they are two distinct types even if they share in the impossibility of coexistence. His statement: (And as for the second) i.e., non-entry into the definition, whether entry necessitates making them a division of opposites or not, for if it were restricted to what necessitates making them a division of opposites, the objection by his statement 'if you say, etc.' would not occur, as is not hidden. His statement: (They are included in the definition absolutely, so it is not correct to make similars absolutely a division of opposites. This is an objection originating from his statement: 'so they are not subsumed under two meanings', and it is not a proof of the premise, i.e., their entry into the definition. His statement: 'So this disagreement is not built, etc.' It is said: The detailed discussion is that among them are those who did not require otherness in similarity and difference, and among them is al-Qāḍī, and among them are those who required it. And those who require it, if they say otherness by attributes, they also describe them with similarity and difference, and if they do not say it, they do not say them either. So the author's intention in his statement 'returns' is an indication of the detail on the condition of otherness, not that the description by otherness is a condition absolutely. So the meaning of his statement 'and among them are those who describe them with both' is the majority, not al-Qāḍī, so that what the commentator mentioned would not occur. And this statement is not far-fetched, except that al-Āmidī did not mention the statement of some about similarity and difference based on the statement of otherness. And God knows best. His statement: (And to this went Shaykh al-Ashʿarī) will come in the Second Objective of the Station of the Divine Matters.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents the Tenth Objective of the Fourth Observatory: the principle that every two similars cannot coexist. The author attributes this view to Shaykh al-Ashʿarī. The discussion begins by noting a possible misunderstanding: one might think that al-Ashʿarī thereby makes two similars a subcategory of opposites (al-mutaḍāddīn), because they seem to fall under the definition of opposites as things that cannot coexist. However, the author refutes this. The truth is that al-Ashʿarī is not obliged to classify them as opposites, nor do they actually enter the definition of opposites. The reason for the impossibility of coexistence is not due to opposition but for another reason to be explained later. Moreover, two similars might be two substances (jawharayn), and the definition of opposites typically applies to two meanings (maʿnayyān, i.e., accidents) such as blackness and whiteness. Therefore, substances are not subsumed under the definition. The commentator (qāla) further explains that the impossibility of coexistence is either due to the absence of a locus (as with substances) or the impossibility of coexistence in a single locus (as with accidents). The author then discusses a binary division: two things either cannot coexist (opposites) or can (different), and the different are further divided into similar and other. The commentary clarifies that the objection about entering the definition is only an apparent objection, not a proof. The passage concludes with a detailed discussion of the role of otherness (al-taghāyur) in similarity and difference, noting that the disagreement about whether attributes can be described as similar or different is based on whether one requires otherness. The author clarifies that his earlier statement 'returns' is an indication of a condition, not an absolute requirement, and that the majority opinion is to describe attributes with similarity and difference, unlike al-Qāḍī.

Key Terms
متماثلان (mutamāthilān)
متضادان (mutaḍāddān)
متخالفان (mutakhālifān)
جوهرين (jawharayn)
عرضين (ʿaraḍayn)
الحد (al-ḥadd)
التغاير (al-taghāyur)
الشيخ الأشعري (Shaykh al-Ashʿarī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because two similars cannot coexist, they must be considered a type of opposite (mutadadd).
Correction: The impossibility of coexistence in similars has a different cause than opposition, and similars can be substances, which do not fit the definition of opposites (which applies to accidents).

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that two similars are not a subcategory of opposites?
A) Because similars never exist in reality.
B) Because the impossibility of their coexistence is due to something other than opposition, and they may be substances which do not fall under the definition of opposites.
C) Because al-Ashʿarī explicitly said they are not opposites.
D) Because opposite is defined as two meanings that cannot coexist in one subject, and similars can be substances.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari principle that two similars cannot coexist does not make them a type of opposite; they are distinct categories with different reasons for impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see two similar ideas or people that seem to clash, do you automatically assume they are opposites, rather than considering they might simply be distinct? In what area of your life can you apply this lesson’s insight not to conflate incompatibility with opposition?
?
This lesson teaches that the impossibility of two similars coexisting stems from the nature of the things—not from rivalry. Think of a situation where you have two similar goals (e.g., career and family). Are you treating them as if they are at war, when perhaps they are just different? What would change if you saw them as distinct rather than opposed?
?
The passage distinguishes between substances and accidents. Are there beliefs or values in your heart that you treat as 'accidents'—changeable and external—when they should be 'substances'—core to your identity? How might this shift your priorities?

Critique of the First Argument Against Reunion of Likes
Critique of first argument against reunion of likes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique of the first argument for the impossibility of the reunion of likes, specifically the distinction between non-distinction in reality and non-distinction from our perspective.

Core Idea
Non-distinction does not equal impossibility; it may just be unknown to us.

In this passage, the author takes aim at the first argument used to prove that two similar things cannot coexist. He argues that the argument's key premise—that non-distinction is impossible—is flawed. Instead, he distinguishes between real non-distinction (which can be avoided by accidental attributes) and perceived non-distinction (which is merely a lack of knowledge).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the author's argument.
Pay attention to the technical terms like عدم التمايز, عوارض, etc.
2
Identify the two claims about non-distinction
The passage discusses non-distinction in two contexts: 'in reality' (في نفس الأمر) and 'for us' (عندنا). Understand how the author treats each.
3
Understand the objection to 'non-distinction in reality'
The author says non-distinction in reality is not impossible because the two likes could be distinguished by accidental attributes from separate causes, not from the locus.
This weakens the argument's foundation.
4
Understand the point about 'non-distinction for us'
The author clarifies that even if we do not perceive distinction, that is just our ignorance—it is not logically impossible.
This shifts the burden back to the opponent.
5
Reflect on the overall critique
Combine the two points to see why the first argument is problematic. The conclusion is that the argument's premise is not necessarily true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد العاشر : كل متماثلين لا يجتمعان
( وفيها ) أي في هذه المسالك كلها ( نظر، فالأول ) : منظور فيه؛ إذ عدم التمايز في نفس الأمر ممتنع ؛ لجواز تمايز المثلين عند الاجتماع بعوارض مستندة إلى أسباب مفارقة دون المحل، (و) عدم التمايز ( عندنا غير ممتنع)؛ مرجعه عدم علمنا بالتمايز ولا محذور فيه .
The Fourth Observatory - The Tenth Intention: Every two similar things cannot coexist. (And in it) i.e., in all these arguments, (there is a problem. The first) is problematic: because non-distinction in reality is impossible due to the possibility of distinction of the two likes at reunion by accidental attributes dependent on separate causes apart from the locus, and non-distinction for us is not impossible; its source is our lack of knowledge of distinction and there is no harm in it.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the author's critique of the first argument used to prove that two similar things cannot coexist. The author states that all the arguments (المسالك) are subject to criticism. Focusing on the first argument, the objection is that the claim of non-distinction between the two likes in reality (في نفس الأمر) is impossible because it is possible that the two likes are distinguished by accidental attributes (عوارض) arising from separate causes (أسباب مفارقة) and not from the locus. However, the author clarifies that non-distinction from our perspective (عندنا) is not impossible—it simply means we do not perceive any distinction, which is harmless. The key point is that the argument's premise about necessary non-distinction is flawed, as distinction remains possible in principle.

Key Terms
عدم التمايز
عوارض
أسباب مفارقة
المحل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-distinction between two similar things is impossible in all respects.
Correction: Non-distinction may be impossible in reality only if accidental attributes are ruled out; for us, non-distinction is merely unknown and not impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection to the first argument for the impossibility of reunion of likes?
A) The two likes are always distinct in reality.
B) Non-distinction is impossible even for us.
C) Non-distinction is possible in reality due to accidental attributes, and for us it is merely unknown.
D) The locus prevents any distinction.

Key Takeaway
The first argument for the impossibility of the reunion of likes fails because non-distinction is not necessarily impossible—it can be due to accidental attributes or simply unknown to us.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a claim that seems impossible because you cannot see how it could be, do you automatically dismiss it? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to equate your lack of perception with objective impossibility? Are there areas of faith where you are waiting for evidence before believing?
?
The author says that non-distinction for us is just ignorance and no harm in it. Have you ever rejected a truth about Allah or the unseen simply because it was beyond your comprehension? What would it mean for you to humbly accept that your knowledge is limited rather than using it as a barrier?
?
Think of a time you insisted two things must be the same because you couldn't tell them apart. Could you have been wrong? How might this humility before unseen distinctions affect your judgments about people, events, or even divine attributes?

Critique of the Second Argument: Universal vs. Particular Negation
Critique of the second argument: it fails to prove the universal negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second argument against the reunion of likes fails to prove its claim and what it actually demonstrates.

Core Idea
The second argument only disproves a specific case, not a universal rule.

In this passage, al-Sharif al-Jurjani critiques the second argument that supposedly shows likes cannot coexist. He points out a critical logical error: the argument confuses a particular impossibility with a universal one. The passage sharpens your ability to distinguish between universal and particular claims in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's critique come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated terms 'السلب الكلي' and 'سلب الكل'.
2
Identify the two key negations
Distinguish between 'universal negation' (السلب الكلي) and 'negation of the universal' (سلب الكل). The former denies all cases; the latter denies that all cases hold.
The author argues the second argument only proves the latter.
3
Understand the example
The argument uses two theoretical sciences (علمين نظريين) about the same object. Ask yourself: why can't they coexist? The author explains it's not because they are likes, but because reflection (النظر) and knowledge (العلم) are incompatible.
4
Reflect on the logical error
Realize that the argument fails to establish the universal claim. The impossibility in the example is due to a specific relation, not the general nature of likes.
This is the core of the critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الثاني منظور فيه، لأنه لا يوجب السلب الكلي الذي هو المدعى أعني قولنا : لا يجوز اجتماع المثلين أصلاً، بل يوجب سلب الكل؛ لأن امتناع اجتماع هذين المثلين أعني العلمين النظريين المتعلقين بمعلوم واحد يوجب رفع الإيجاب الكلي، أعني قولنا : ليس كل مثلين يجوز اجتماعهما، وليس بمطلوب ولا بمستلزم له؛ إذ ليس امتناع اجتماعهما لكونهما مثلين بل لأن النظر لا يجامع العلم بما ينظر فيه على ما سلف .
And similarly the second is problematic, because it does not entail the universal negation which is the claim, i.e., our statement: 'It is not permissible for likes to coexist at all,' but it entails the negation of the universal; because the impossibility of coexistence of these two likes, i.e., the two theoretical sciences related to one object of knowledge, entails the raising of the universal affirmation, i.e., our statement: 'Not every two likes may coexist,' which is neither the goal nor entailing it; since the impossibility of their coexistence is not because they are likes, but because theoretical reflection does not accompany knowledge of what is reflected upon, as has preceded.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage critiques the second argument. It points out that the argument, which uses two theoretical sciences (علمين نظريين) about the same object, does not prove the universal negative (السلب الكلي) that 'likes can never coexist.' At most, it proves the negation of the universal (سلب الكل), meaning 'not every pair of likes can coexist.' The reason is that the impossibility here is not due to similarity (تماثل) but because theoretical reflection (النظر) and knowledge (العلم) of the same object are incompatible. This is a specific case, not a general rule. Therefore, the argument fails to support the claim that all likes cannot coexist.

Key Terms
السلب الكلي
سلب الكل
الإيجاب الكلي
النظر
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second argument proves that all likes cannot coexist universally.
Correction: The argument only shows that a specific pair of likes cannot coexist due to other reasons, not because of their likeness itself. This is not a universal proof.

Checkpoint
What does the author say the second argument actually proves?
A) That no two likes can ever coexist.
B) That the impossibility of coexistence is due to similarity.
C) That not every pair of likes can coexist, but not that none can.
D) That the argument is valid and conclusive.

Key Takeaway
The second argument fails because it only shows that a specific pair of likes cannot coexist due to other reasons, not because of their likeness itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that a single counterexample disproves an entire rule in your own beliefs? When have you done this in matters of faith, and what was the result? Are you willing to check your assumptions against the logical distinctions shown here, even if it challenges your comfort?
?
This lesson shows that the impossibility in the example was not due to likeness but due to an accidental cause. Are there areas in your life where you have attributed a conflict to an essential difference (e.g., between people, ideas, or practices) when the real cause was circumstantial? Will you investigate that today, even if it means letting go of a cherished narrative?
?
The author critiques an argument that fails to prove its claim. When you argue for a truth you hold, do you ensure your reasoning is valid and not just emotionally satisfying? If you discovered your own argument was flawed like the second argument here, would you be honest enough to abandon it and seek a better foundation? Commit to one specific belief you hold that you will examine with this same rigor before the end of this week.

Critique of the Third Argument Against Reunion of Likes
Author critiques the third argument, questioning its premises about vacuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author critiques the third argument by denying its two premises regarding vacuity of the locus and the impossibility of emptiness of both a thing and its opposite.

Core Idea
The third argument collapses because its two premises are both deniable.

In this passage, the author continues his critical examination of arguments against the reunion of likes. He turns to the third argument, which relies on two key premises about the possibility of a locus being empty of one like. The author shows that neither premise is necessarily true, and then responds to a potential objection. This lesson walks through the critique step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises of the third argument
The third argument is built on two premises: (1) that the locus can be empty of one of the two likes, and (2) that the locus cannot be empty of a thing and its opposite. Locate these in the passage.
These premises are stated in the first line of the Arabic.
2
See how the author denies each premise
The author provides counterexamples: For premise 1, the two likes might be necessary to the locus, so they cannot be removed. For premise 2, the locus could be empty of both the departed like and its opposite, so no contradiction arises.
Notice that the author does not just reject — he provides a logical possibility.
3
Follow the objection and the author's reply
The opponent retorts that even if the like departs, its absence makes the locus suitable for the opposite. The author replies that the remaining like prevents that suitability.
This shows the back-and-forth structure of kalam disputation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الثالث منظور فيه ; لأنه فرع جواز الخلو أي خلو المحل الذي اجتمع فيه المثلان عن أحدهما، وفرع أن المحل لا يخلو عن الشيء وضده، وكلاهما ممنوع، أما الأول فلجواز أن يكون المثلان المجتمعان في محل لازمين له، فلا يجوز زوال شيء منهما عنه، وأما الثاني : فلجواز أن يخلو المحل عن الشيء الذي هو المثل الزائل وعن ضده أيضاً، فلا يلزم اجتماع الضدين فإن قلت : نحن نقول أن انتفاء أحد المثلين عن المحل يصحح اتصافه بضده فيلزم جواز اجتماع المتضادين قطعاً، ولا حاجة بنا إلى وقوعه . قلت : لا نسلم أيضاً كون ذلك الانتفاء مصححاً للضد؛ وجود المثل الباقي .
And similarly the third is problematic; because it is premised on the possibility of vacuity, i.e., the vacuum of the locus in which the two likes have gathered from one of them, and premised on the fact that the locus is not empty of the thing and its opposite, and both are denied. As for the first, it is possible that the two likes gathered in a locus are necessary to it, so removal of any of them is not permissible. As for the second, it is possible that the locus may be emptied of the thing which is the departed like and also of its opposite, so the coexistence of the two contraries does not follow. For if you say: we say that the non-existence of one of the likes in the locus renders it suitable for being characterised by its opposite, so the permissibility of coexistence of the two contraries necessarily follows, and we have no need for its actual occurrence. I say: we also do not grant that that non-existence makes it suitable for the opposite, given the existence of the remaining like.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage critiques the third argument, which is based on two premises: (1) that the locus can be empty of one of the two likes (جواز الخلو), and (2) that the locus cannot be empty of both a thing and its opposite (لا يخلو عن الشيء وضده). The author denies both premises. For the first, it is possible that the two likes are necessary attributes of the locus, so they cannot be removed. For the second, it is possible that the locus is void of both the departed like and its opposite, so no contradiction arises. The opponent then retorts that even if the like departs, the absence of that like makes the locus suitable for the opposite, thus allowing the coexistence of two contraries. The author replies that this suitability is not granted because the remaining like still occupies the locus, preventing the opposite from entering.

Key Terms
جواز الخلو
لازم
ضد
انتفاء
مصحح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third argument assumes the locus can always be vacant of one like and that the locus cannot be empty of both a thing and its opposite, so the argument is sound.
Correction: The author shows that both premises are deniable: likes may be necessary (so no vacuity) and the locus may be empty of both like and opposite, so no contradiction arises.

Checkpoint
Which premise of the third argument does the author deny first?
A) That the locus cannot be empty of a thing and its opposite.
B) That the two likes are necessary to the locus.
C) That the locus can be empty of one of the likes.
D) That non-existence makes the locus suitable for the opposite.

Key Takeaway
The third argument against reunion of likes fails because its key premises about vacuity and emptiness of opposites are not necessarily true, as likes may be inseparable and the locus may be empty of both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author takes nothing for granted, not even seemingly obvious premises like 'the locus cannot be empty of both a thing and its opposite'. In your own faith, what is one belief you hold that you have never actually examined from its foundations? This week, set aside time to question that belief honestly—not to destroy it, but to see if it can withstand scrutiny.
?
When the opponent objects, the author replies firmly, 'We do not grant that.' Do you have the same courage to refuse assumptions that contradict your understanding of the truth—even when they seem plausible? Think of a recent doubt or challenge to your beliefs. Did you grant it too quickly? What would it mean to 'not grant' it until it has been proven from revelation or reason?
?
This lesson is about a logical argument in kalam, but the method applies to your soul. The author shows that having two likes (perhaps analogous to two good things) in one locus is not automatically problematic, and the third argument fails. Reflect: Is there a tension in your heart between two good things you think you cannot have at once—like devotion to family and devotion to Allah? Have you assumed they cannot coexist because of a faulty argument? Commit to seeking a way to reconcile them, trusting that God's order allows for harmony.

Responding to the Fourth Argument
Author critiques the fourth argument and illustrates with dyeing example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author responds to the fourth argument by accepting its consequence without conceding the opponent's position.

Core Idea
Accepting a consequence does not mean the argument disproves your position.

Sanusi critiques the fourth argument, which claims that if reunion of likes were possible, we could never be certain that a particular blackness is one. The author responds by accepting that consequence, but shows it does not refute the Mu'tazila's position. The example of a body dipped in dye illustrates how multiple similar qualities can appear successively, undermining the argument's force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's words come through.
2
Identify the fourth argument's claim
The fourth argument claims that without the impossibility of reunion, we lose certainty that a quality in a locus is one. Recognize this as the claim being critiqued.
3
Note the author's response
The author says 'we commit' (نلتزم) to not being certain. This is a dialectical acceptance, not a concession.
The author accepts the consequence but says it is for the Mu'tazila's proof.
4
Examine the dyeing example
The example of a body dipped in dye shows successive colors: turbidity, grey, black, intense black. This illustrates that multiple similar qualities can appear, challenging the argument.
The example supports the permissibility of reunion.
5
Understand the dialectical strategy
Accepting a consequence does not mean the opponent's argument succeeds. The author shows the argument does not achieve its goal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع أيضاً منظور فيه ; للالتزام أي نلتزم أنه لا يمكننا الجزم يكون السواد القائم بالمحل المعين واحداً لهم أي للمعتزلة في إثبات جواز الاجتماع الجسم يغمس في الصبغ فيعلوه، كدرة ثم كهبة ثم سواد، ثم حلوكة .
And the fourth is also problematic; because of the commitment, i.e., we commit that we cannot be certain that the blackness subsisting in a particular locus is one, for them, i.e., for the Mu'tazila, in proving the permissibility of reunion: a body immersed in dye is covered by it, first turbidity, then greyness, then blackness, then intense blackness.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage critiques the fourth argument, which argues that if reunion of likes were possible, we could never be certain that the blackness in a particular locus is one and unique. The author replies by accepting this commitment: indeed, we cannot be certain of that unity. But that does not refute the claim of the Mu'tazila, who seek to prove the permissibility of reunion. The example of a body dipped in dye is given, where successive colors appear (turbidity, grey, black, intense black) – presumably illustrating that multiple similar qualities can coexist or succeed each other. The critique implies that the argument does not achieve its goal: it does not prove the impossibility of reunion of likes.

Key Terms
الالتزام
الجزم
السواد
الجسم
الصبغ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If you accept the consequence of an argument, you must admit the argument is valid.
Correction: Accepting a consequence does not mean the argument proves its claim; it may simply be irrelevant or insufficient.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the fourth argument?
A) He denies that we lose certainty.
B) He accepts the consequence but says it does not prove the opponent's case.
C) He proves that certainty is still possible.
D) He rejects the example of dyeing.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument fails because accepting its consequence does not disprove the opponent's position.

?

The Coexistence of Similar Things: Why Similarity Does Not Prevent Combination
States the general principle of possible coexistence and refines the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that similarity alone does not necessitate impossibility of coexistence, and that the true reason in the case of knowledge and consideration is the incompatibility of simultaneous knowing and considering.

Core Idea
Similarity does not cause impossibility – the cause is the incompatibility of knowledge and consideration.

In this passage, the author addresses a common assumption: that two similar things cannot coexist. He immediately corrects this, stating that similarity itself does not forbid conjunction. Instead, he introduces a nuanced argument about the relationship between the act of consideration and the knowledge it produces, drawing on earlier discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation carefully to grasp the surface meaning.
Notice that the passage has two main parts: the general principle and the specific clarification.
2
Identify the distinction between similarity and real cause
The author states that the impossibility of combining two similar things is not due to their similarity. Find the phrase that gives the real reason.
The real reason is that consideration does not accompany the knowledge that comes from it.
3
Analyze the commentary part
The passage includes a commentary (قوله) that clarifies what 'does not accompany knowledge' means. Understand the distinction between the specific knowledge resulting from consideration and general knowledge as a prerequisite.
This clears up a potential misunderstanding: general knowledge is needed for consideration, but the resulting knowledge is incompatible with the act of considering.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل مثلين يجوز اجتماعهما، وليس بمطلوب ولا بمستلزم له؛ إذ ليس امتناع اجتماعهما لكونهما مثلين بل لأن النظر لا يجامع العلم بما ينظر فيه على ما سلف . قوله : لا بجامع العلم بما ينظر فيه أي بالوجه الذي يحصل من النظر، وإلا فالعلم بالمنظور فيه في الجملة شرط للنظر، لامتناع طلب المجهول المطلق.
Every two similar things may be combined, and it is neither required nor necessitated; for the impossibility of their combination is not because they are similar, but because consideration does not accompany knowledge of what is considered, as previously stated. His statement: 'It does not accompany knowledge of what is considered' means by the aspect that occurs from consideration; otherwise, knowledge of the considered object in general is a condition for consideration, because seeking the absolutely unknown is impossible.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage introduces a key point in the discussion of whether two similar things can coexist. The author first asserts a general principle: any two similar things may possibly coexist. He then clarifies that this point is not the primary claim sought (مطلوب) in the argument, nor is it a necessary consequence (مستلزم) of it. The reason the Mu'tazilites think two similar things cannot coexist is not because of their similarity per se, but because the act of considering (نظر) something cannot coexist with already knowing it. The commentator (قوله) then explains that when the author says 'it does not accompany knowledge', he means the specific knowledge that arises from consideration; otherwise, general knowledge of the subject is a prerequisite for any consideration, since one cannot seek something absolutely unknown.

Key Terms
مثلين
جواز
مطلوب
مستلزم
امتناع
نظر
علم
مجهول مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of two similar things coexisting is due to their similarity itself.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the impossibility is not because they are similar, but because of the incompatibility between consideration and the knowledge it produces.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do two similar things not actually coexist in the case of knowledge and consideration?
A) Because they are similar
B) Because consideration cannot accompany the knowledge that results from it
C) Because seeking the unknown is impossible
D) Because the consideration itself is the object of knowledge

Key Takeaway
Similarity does not inherently prevent coexistence; the impossibility arises from the relationship between knowledge and consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided pursuing a deeper understanding of a truth because you assumed two similar ideas couldn't both be valid? This lesson shows that similarity is not the barrier—what is the real barrier in your own heart when you stop seeking? Are you willing to admit that your reluctance might be because you already think you know?
?
The passage says 'seeking the absolutely unknown is impossible.' When you approach the study of Allah's attributes or matters of faith, do you build on what you know or do you pretend to start from nothing? What would change if you acknowledged the knowledge you already have as a condition for deeper consideration?
?
The impossibility here is not in the things but in the act of consideration. In your daily life, do you mistake the inability to hold two thoughts at once for proof that both cannot be true? How can you train yourself to humbly separate your mental limitations from objective reality?

The Dyeing Argument and Its Refutation
Presents the Mu'tazilite argument using dyeing and refutes it by distinguishing color degrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite dyeing argument for the coexistence of similars and the Sunni refutation based on successive replacement of colors differing in intensity.

Core Idea
Colors replace each other; they do not accumulate.

The Mu'tazilites used a vivid example—dyeing a cloth repeatedly—to argue that multiple similar blacknesses can coexist. In this passage, al-Sanusi presents their claim and then refutes it by showing that the observed shades are not multiple blacknesses but a sequence of distinct colors that differ in intensity and replace each other.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's presentation of the argument and response sink in.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
Notice how they describe the progressive shades: turbidity, dust-color, blackness, deep black. They claim each shade is composed of multiple units of blackness, proving coexistence of similars.
Identify the key terms: كدرة, كهبة, سواد, حلوكة.
3
Grasp the refutation
The answer says these colors differ in intensity and weakness, and they succeed one another (the second removes the first). They do not coexist. The illusion of accumulation comes from the increasing intensity.
Focus on the phrase 'تتوارد هذه الألوان على الجسم بدلاً'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع أيضاً منظور فيه؛ للالتزام أي نلتزم أنه لا يمكننا الجزم يكون السواد القائم بالمحل المعين واحداً لهم أي للمعتزلة في إثبات جواز الاجتماع الجسم يغمس في الصبغ فيعلوه كدرة ثم كهبة ثم سواد، ثم حلوكة، وليس ذلك الاختلاف في لونه بحسب تكرير الغمس إلا لتضاعف أفراد السواد المطلق عليه، فالكهبة كدرتان اجتمعتا والسواد كهبتان والحلوكة سوادان، فثبت اجتماع المثلين. والجواب: أن كل واحد منها أي من الألوان المذكورة لون مخالف للآخر في الشدة والضعف، وتتوارد هذه الألوان على الجسم بدلاً، وبالثاني يزول الأول عنه، ولا يتصور اجتماعها في ذلك الجسم أصلاً، إلا أنه لما كان المتأخر أشد من المتقدم في السوادية توهم أن فيه اجتماع لونين متماثلين.
And the fourth is also under scrutiny; because of the commitment—i.e., we commit that we cannot be certain that the blackness subsisting in a particular subject is one—for them, i.e., for the Mu'tazilites, in proving the possibility of combination: the body is immersed in dye and it becomes turbidity (kudra), then dust-color (kuhba), then blackness (sawad), then deep blackness (huluka). The variation in its color due to repeated immersion is only because of the multiplication of individuals of absolute blackness upon it. So dust-color is two turbidities combined, and blackness is two dust-colors, and deep blackness is two blacknesses. Thus the combination of two like things is proven. The answer: each one of these colors is a color different from the other in intensity and weakness, and they succeed upon the body in alternation, and by the second the first disappears from it. Their combination in that body is not conceivable at all. However, because the later is more intense in blackness than the earlier, one imagines that there is a combination of two similar colors.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth Mu'tazilite argument for the possibility of two similar things coexisting, using the example of dye. They argue that when a body is repeatedly dipped in dye, it goes through shades: turbidity (كدرة), then a color between dust and black (كهبة), then black (سواد), then deep black (حلوكة). The Mu'tazilites claim that these are not just different shades but actually multiple individuals of blackness accumulating, so that dust-color is two turbidities, blackness is two dust-colors, etc., proving coexistence of similars. The answer is that each of these colors is different by degree of intensity; they do not coexist but replace each other. Because the later is more intense, one mistakenly imagines two similar colors are present together when in fact only one color exists at a time.

Key Terms
رابع
التزام
صبغ
كدرة
كهبة
سواد
حلوكة
توارد
بدل
شدة
ضعف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the progressive shades in dyeing are multiple similar blacknesses coexisting in the same subject.
Correction: Each shade is a single color of varying intensity; they replace one another and do not coexist.

Checkpoint
How does the answer refute the Mu'tazilite claim that multiple blackness individuals coexist?
A) By asserting that all colors are the same
B) By stating that colors vary in intensity and replace each other successively
C) By denying that the body changes color
D) By claiming the dye is not real

Key Takeaway
The dyeing example fails because each shade is a distinct color differing in intensity, and they replace each other, not accumulate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you assessing your spiritual growth as merely adding more good deeds, when what may be needed is a replacement of weaker iman with stronger iman? Which 'shade' of faith do you currently have, and what would it take to allow a deeper one to replace it?
?
The Mu'tazilites were deceived by the appearance of accumulation. Where in your own life might you be mistaking a gradual change in intensity for a change in kind—especially in your character or worship? What would it mean to let an old 'color' completely disappear so a new one can take its place?
?
Consider an area where you feel your devotion has grown stronger. Are you certain that the stronger devotion hasn't simply replaced the weaker one, or do you assume the weaker still remains? How would your actions change if you truly believed that God wants complete replacement, not accumulation?

The Two Opposites and the Three Unities
Definition of opposites with three conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the two opposites and why the three conditions of unity (time, subject, aspect) are necessary.

Core Idea
Opposites cannot coexist in one time, one subject, and one aspect.

This passage from the philosophers establishes the foundational definition of opposites (al-mutaqabilan) and clarifies the precise conditions under which opposition is meaningful. While the term 'coexistence' already implies simultaneity, the author explains why each condition—time, subject, and aspect—must be explicitly stated to avoid confusion, especially regarding correlatives like paternity and sonship.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' definition
Start by reading the Arabic passage once. Notice the definition: 'The two opposites are two things that do not coexist in one time.'
This definition sets the stage for the three conditions.
2
Understand why unity of time is explicit
The author argues that 'coexistence' (ijtimā') already implies simultaneity, but the condition of time is added to prevent a metaphorical usage that would allow opposites in one essence across different times.
This shows precision in technical definitions.
3
Recognize the implicit unity of subject
Although not explicitly stated in the quoted definition, the condition of unity of subject (dhāt) is inherent: opposites cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time. The passage clarifies that coexistence in two subjects is permissible.
Unity of subject is often taken for granted but is implied.
4
Grasp the necessity of unity of aspect
The third condition, unity of aspect (jiha), is explicitly added to include correlatives (mutaḍāyifān) like paternity and sonship, which can coexist in one person from different aspects (e.g., being a father and a son simultaneously).
This condition prevents false accusations of opposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الحادي عشر : المتقابلان أمران لا يجتمعان

قال الحكماء المتقابلان أمران لا يجتمعان في زمان واحد

لا شك أن المتبادر من لفظ الاجتماع ما يغني عن قيد وحدة الزمان إلا أنه قد يقال : ولو على سبيل المجاز، اجتمع هذان الوصفان في ذات واحدة وإن كانا في وقتين فصرح بوحدته دفعاً لتوهم التجوز في الاجتماع في ذات واحدة؛ لأن اجتماع المتقابلين في زمان واحد في ذاتين جائز، من جهة واحدة هذا القيد الأخير أعني وحدة الجهة؛ لإدخال المتضايفين، كالأبوة والبنوة العارضين لزيد من جهتين
The Fourth Observatory - The Eleventh Objective: The two opposites are two things that do not coexist in one time.

The philosophers said: The two opposites are two things that do not coexist in one time.

There is no doubt that the apparent meaning of the word 'ijtimā'' (combination) suffices from the condition of unity of time, except that it might be said, even metaphorically: 'These two attributes coexisted in one essence while being in two times.' So he explicitly stated its unity to repel the suspicion of using 'ijtimā'' metaphorically in one essence; because the coexistence of the two opposites in one time in two essences is permissible, and (he also explicitly stated) from one aspect, this last condition meaning unity of aspect, to include the correlatives (mutaḍāyifān), like paternity and sonship that occur to Zayd from two aspects.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines the two opposites (al-mutaqābilān) and explains the conditions for their coexistence. The author begins by quoting the philosophers' definition: 'The two opposites are two things that do not coexist in one time.' The commentator then notes that the word 'ijtimā'' (coexistence) itself implies simultaneity, so the condition of unity of time might seem redundant. However, to prevent the metaphorical use of 'ijtimā'' for mere occurrence in a single essence over different times, the condition is explicitly stated. The condition of unity of subject (dhāt) is also implicit, but the commentator clarifies that coexistence in one essence in one time is impossible for opposites, while in two essences it is possible. Additionally, the condition of unity of aspect (jiha) is stated to include correlatives like paternity and sonship, which can coexist in the same person from different aspects.

Key Terms
متقابلان (mutaqābilān)
اجتماع (ijtimā')
ذات (dhāt)
زمان (zamān)
جهة (jiha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The condition 'in one time' is redundant because 'coexistence' already means 'at the same time'.
Correction: The explicit mention of time prevents metaphorical interpretations where attributes are said to 'coexist' in a single essence across different times.

Checkpoint
Why does the author explicitly mention 'fī zamān wāḥid' in the definition of opposites?
A) Because it is the only condition
B) To prevent metaphorical interpretation of 'ijtimā''
C) To exclude correlatives
D) To ensure unity of subject

Key Takeaway
The definition of opposites includes three unities—time, subject, and aspect—to avoid ambiguity and include all cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you held two opposing beliefs or attitudes about the same person or situation. In what aspect were you inconsistent? Will you commit to examining one area of your life where your actions contradict your stated faith, and seek to align them before Allah?
?
The philosophers insist on unity of time, subject, and aspect for opposites. Apply this to your own heart: Are there good and bad qualities that you allow to coexist in you because you change the 'aspect' or 'time'? What would it take to truly confront one such inconsistency today?
?
This lesson defines opposites by what they cannot do: coexist. How does your inability to serve both Allah and your desires (a true opposition) reveal the sincerity of your worship? Are you trying to combine them in different times or aspects? When will you choose one master?
