Non-Negation Opposites: Correlatives and Contraries
He classifies non-negation opposites into correlatives and contraries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how non-negation opposites are classified into correlatives and contraries based on whether conception of each depends on the other.

Core Idea
Non-negation opposites are either correlatives (conceptually interdependent) or contraries (not conceptually interdependent).

In this passage, the author continues the discussion of opposites by focusing on those that are not simple negations. He further divides these into two types: correlatives, where each is understood only in relation to the other, and contraries, where no such reciprocal conception is required. The commentary clarifies the nature of dependence and why these contraries are called 'popular'.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the main division: opposites are either negations or non-negations; non-negations split into correlatives and contraries.
2
Understand the definition of correlatives
Note that correlatives (mutaḍāyifān) are opposites where each is conceived only by reference to the other, like father and son. Their conception depends on the other.
The commentary emphasizes that this dependence is in conception, not just necessary implication.
3
Contrast with contraries
Contraries (ḍiddān) are opposites where conception does not depend on the other. Examples include black and white, which can be conceived independently.
These are called 'popular contraries' because they are well-known among philosophers.
4
Examine the commentator's clarification
The commentator explains that for correlatives, the dependence is part of the definition, not just a necessary concomitant. He also justifies negating dependence in the definition of contraries while not negating necessary implication.
This clarifies the precise logical relationship.
5
Reflect on the classification scheme
Consider how this classification fits into the broader study of opposites. Recognize that opposites not involving negation are exhaustively divided into correlatives and contraries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإما أن لا يكون أحدهما سلباً للآخر أو يكون، والأول ينقسم إلى قسمين، لأنه إن لم يعقل كل منهما إلا بالقياس إلى الآخر، فهما المتضايفان، وسيأتي بيان أحوالهما في آخر الموقف الثالث. وإلا فهما الضدان، وعلى هذا فتعريفهما أنهما متقابلان ليس أحدهما سلباً للآخر، ولا يتوقف تعقل كل منهما على صاحبه، وهما بهذا المعنى يسميان ضدين مشهورين

قوله : ( إلا بالقياس إلى الآخر قال المصنف في بحث الإضافة: قولهم المضاف ما تعقل ماهيته بالقياس إلى الغير لا يراد به أنه يلزم من تعقله تعقل الغير فإن اللوازم البينة كذلك، بل أن يكون من حقيقته تعقل الغير، فلا يتم تعقله إلا بتعقل الغير، فعلم أنه لكونه نسبة متكررة يتوقف تعقل كل منهما على تعقل صاحبه، فلذا نفى التوقف في تعريف الضدين دون الاستلزام. قوله : (ضدين مشهورين لاشتهاره بین عوام الفلاسفة، كذا قال الشيخ.
Thus either one of them is not the negation of the other, or it is. And the first is divided into two sections: Because if each of them is not conceived except by reference to the other, then they are the two correlatives (mutaḍāyifān), and an explanation of their states will come at the end of the Third Observatory. Otherwise they are the two contraries (ḍiddān). And on this, their definition is that they are two opposites, one of them is not the negation of the other, and the conception of each does not depend on the other. And they are, in this sense, called popular contraries (ḍiddān mashhūriyān).

His saying: (except by reference to the other) — the author said in the discussion of relation: 'Their statement: the relative (muḍāf) is that whose quiddity is conceived by reference to another does not mean that the conception of the other necessarily follows from its conception, for necessary concomitants are like that; rather, that the conception of the other is part of its reality, so its conception is not completed except by conception of the other. So it is known that because it is a repeated relation, the conception of each depends on the conception of the other. Therefore, he negated dependence in the definition of the contraries, but not the necessary implication.' 
His saying: (popular contraries) because of their fame among the common philosophers, as the shaykh said.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage classifies opposites that are not negations of each other. The author first divides opposites into two kinds: those where one is the negation of the other (salb) and those where it is not. The latter is further divided into two: (1) correlatives (mutaḍāyifān), where each is conceived only by reference to the other, like father and son; and (2) contraries (ḍiddān), where conception does not depend on the other. The commentator clarifies that for correlatives, the dependence is in conception, not just necessary implication. The contraries in this sense are called 'popular' (mashhūrī) because they are commonly known among philosophers. The passage emphasizes that for contraries, the definition does not require reciprocal conception.

Key Terms
سلب (salb)
متضايفان (mutaḍāyifān)
ضدان (ḍiddān)
مفهوم (mafhūm)
توقف (tawaqquf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes correlatives from contraries?
A) Correlatives require reciprocal conception
B) Correlatives are always existent
C) Contraries require negation
D) Contraries are always popular

Key Takeaway
Non-negation opposites are either correlatives (conceptually interdependent) or contraries (not conceptually interdependent).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated a relationship in your life (like parent-child or teacher-student) as if it were a mere contrary—independent and unrelated—rather than a correlative where your identity is tied to the other? How has that affected your interactions? Will you begin to see that relationship as mutually defining?
?
In your own understanding of truth and falsehood, do you consider them as correlatives (each gains meaning only in reference to the other) or as independent contraries? How does that perspective shape your search for truth? Commit to examine one belief you hold and ask yourself: could this belief even be conceived without its opposite?
?
The passage distinguishes between necessary implication and essential dependence. In your faith, are there doctrines you treat as merely implied by other beliefs, rather than recognizing them as essential to your understanding? How might your devotion deepen if you saw them as interdependent correlatives?

Condition of Utmost Difference in Contraries
Distinguishes popular from true contraries by condition of utmost difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition of utmost difference that distinguishes real contraries from popular ones and how this affects the classification of opposites into four categories.

Core Idea
True contraries require maximum difference; otherwise they are merely opponents.

This passage refines the definition of contraries by introducing the condition of utmost difference. Some opposites, like black and white, are maximally different, while others, like red and yellow, are not and are termed opponents (mutanā'idān). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly classifying opposites in logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Pay attention to the examples of black/white and red/yellow.
2
Identify the condition of utmost difference
Notice that the passage stipulates 'utmost difference and distance' (ghāyat al-khilāf) for certain contraries. Contrast the examples to see which pairs satisfy this condition.
3
Understand the popular vs. real distinction
The passage introduces two senses of opposition: popular (mashhūrī) which includes mutanā'idān, and real (ḥaqīqī) which requires utmost difference. See how this affects the division of opposites into four categories.
The commentator adds that even popular opposition may require utmost difference, and that one contrary may be the privation of the other.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this distinction resolves potential ambiguities in classifying opposites. Ask yourself why this condition matters for logical reasoning.
This connects to the broader structure of logical dichotomies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يشترط في الضدين أن يكون بينهما غاية الخلاف والبعد كالسواد والبياض فإنهما متخالفان متباعدان في الغاية، دون الحمرة والصفرة إذ ليس بينهما ولا بين أحدهما وبين السواد والبياض ذلك الخلاف والتباعد فيسميان بالمتعاندين والضدان بهذا المعنى يسميان بالحقيقيين، فإن اعتبر في تقسيم المتقابلين إلى الأقسام الأربعة التضاد المشهوري الشامل للتعاند، فذاك، وإن اعتبر الحقيقي وجب جعل المتعاندين قسماً

قوله : ( التضاد المشهوري الشامل إلخ) يسمى هذا التضاد بالمشهوري؛ لكونه المشهور فيما بين عوام الفلاسفة، ويسمى المعنى الخاص بالتضاد الحقيقي؛ لكونه المعتبر في علومهم الحقيقية. وقد يقال : الشيخ صرح باشتراط غاية الخلاف في التضاد المشهوري أيضا، وحينئذ يكون تقابل مثل السواد والصفرة خارجاً عن الأقسام الأربعة البتة. وصرح أيضاً بأن الضدين في التضاد المشهوري لا يلزم أن يكونا موجودين، بل قد يكون أحدهما عدماً للآخر فهو لا يكون قسيماً لتقابل العدم والملكة وتقابل السلب والإيجاب.
And it may be stipulated in the two contraries that there be between them utmost difference and distance, like black and white, for they are dissimilar and extremely distant. Not so red and yellow, for there is not between them nor between one of them and black and white that difference and distance, so they are called 'mutanā'idān' (opposed). And the two contraries in this sense are called real (ḥaqīqī). So if the popular opposition (taḍād mashhūrī) that includes the 'mutanā'idān' is considered in the division of opposites into the four categories, then that; and if the real opposition is considered, then it is necessary to make the 'mutanā'idān' a separate category.

His saying: (the popular opposition that includes etc.) — this opposition is called popular because of its fame among the common philosophers; and the specific meaning is called real opposition because it is the one considered in their real sciences. And it may be said: The shaykh explicitly stated the condition of utmost difference in popular opposition as well, and in that case the opposition like black and yellow falls outside the four categories entirely. And he also explicitly stated that the two contraries in popular opposition do not necessarily have to be existent; rather, one of them may be the privation of the other, so it is not a division of the opposition of privation and possession (taqābul al-‘adam wa al-milka) and the opposition of affirmation and negation (taqābul al-salb wa al-ījāb).
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage refines the definition of contraries by introducing the condition of utmost difference (ghāyat al-khilāf). The author states that some scholars stipulate that true contraries, like black and white, must be maximally different. Others, like red and yellow, are not so distant and are called 'mutanā'idān' (opponents). The contraries with utmost difference are called 'real' (ḥaqīqī). The passage then discusses how this affects the division of opposites into four categories: if we use popular opposition (which includes mutanā'idān), then the four categories stand; if we use real opposition, mutanā'idān become a separate category. The commentator adds that even popular opposition may require utmost difference, and that in popular opposition, one contrary may be a privation, which affects the classification.

Key Terms
غاية الخلاف (ghāyat al-khilāf)
تضاد مشهوري (taḍād mashhūrī)
تضاد حقيقي (taḍād ḥaqīqī)
متعاندان (muta'āndān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All contraries require utmost difference.
Correction: Only real contraries require utmost difference; popular contraries include mutanā'idān which lack it.

Checkpoint
According to the real definition, how are opposites like red and yellow classified?
A) Real contraries
B) Popular contraries
C) Muta'āndān
D) Not opposites

Key Takeaway
The condition of utmost difference separates real contraries from mere opponents, impacting how opposites are categorized in logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a disagreement you have with someone that you believe is a fundamental 'contrary' (utmost difference). Is it possible that you have mistaken a mere 'opponent' (lesser difference) for a real contrary? What would change if you admitted the difference is not as deep?
?
The passage distinguishes between popular and real definitions. Which of your own strongly held beliefs might be 'popular' rather than 'real'? How can you test their foundation without relying on common opinion?
?
Imagine you are in a theological debate. If you treat the opposing view as a 'real contrary' (utmost difference), you might close off dialogue. How would your approach change if you saw it as a 'mutanā'id' (opponent) within a broader category? Can you point to a specific issue where this applies?

Primary Division of Opposites: Correlatives and Contraries
The author defines the two primary divisions of opposites: correlatives and contraries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two primary categories of opposition in logic: correlatives and contraries, and how they are distinguished by the dependence of intellection.

Core Idea
Opposites are divided by whether each is understood only through the other.

Before delving into more complex logical relations, we must first grasp the basic division of opposites. The author introduces correlatives, which are understood relative to each other, and contraries, which are not. This passage establishes the foundation for understanding opposition in classical logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the definition.
The gloss clarifies the meaning of 'except in relation to the other'.
2
Identify the two types of opposites
Notice that opposites are divided into two types: correlatives and contraries. The distinction is based on whether intellection of each depends on the other.
3
Understand the key distinction: dependence of intellection
In correlatives, each is understood only in relation to the other. In contraries, this is not the case. The gloss explains why dependence in correlatives is essential, not accidental.
The author contrasts 'necessary concomitant' with 'part of the quiddity'.
4
Note the definition of contraries
Contraries are defined as opposites where neither is the negation of the other and intellection of each does not depend on its companion.
This excludes correlatives and also excludes contradictories (negations).
5
Reflect on examples
Think of examples: father/son are correlatives; black/white are contraries. Why does each fit its category?
This reinforces the conceptual distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسمين، لأنه ( إن لم يعقل كل منهما إلا بالقياس إلى الآخر، فهما المتضايفان، وسيأتي بيان أحوالهما في آخر الموقف الثالث . ( وإلا فهما الضدان)، وعلى هذا فتعريفهما أنهما متقابلان ليس أحدهما سلباً للآخر، ولا يتوقف تعقل كل منهما على صاحبه، وهما بهذا المعنى يسميان ضدين مشهورين . قوله : ( إلا بالقياس إلى الآخر قال المصنف في بحث الإضافة: قولهم المضاف ما تعقل ماهيته بالقياس إلى الغير لا يراد به أنه يلزم من تعقله تعقل الغير فإن اللوازم البينة كذلك، بل أن يكون من حقيقته تعقل الغير، فلا يتم تعقله إلا بتعقل الغير، فعلم أنه لكونه نسبة متكررة يتوقف تعقل كل منهما على تعقل صاحبه، فلذا نفى التوقف في تعريف الضدين دون الاستلزام.
Two divisions: because if each one of them is not intellected except in relation to the other, then they are the two correlatives (al-mutadāyifān), and the exposition of their states will come at the end of the third station (al-mawqif al-thālith). Otherwise, they are the two contraries (al-ḍiddān). And on this basis, their definition is that they are two opposites, neither of which is a negation of the other, and the intellection of each one does not depend on its companion. And they are in this sense called the two well-known contraries (ḍiddān mashhūriyyān). His saying: 'except in relation to the other'—the author said in the discussion of relation: their saying 'the relative is that whose quiddity is intellected in relation to another' does not mean that the intellection of another necessarily follows from its intellection, for the evident necessary concomitants are like that. Rather, it means that the intellection of another is part of its reality, so its intellection is not completed except by intellection of the other. Thus it is known that because it is a repeated relation, the intellection of each depends on the intellection of its companion. Hence he negated dependence in the definition of the two contraries but not necessary concomitance.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage defines the two primary types of opposition: correlatives (al-mutadāyifān) and contraries (al-ḍiddān). The author states that opposites are divided into two kinds: if each is intellected only by reference to the other, they are correlatives (e.g., father and son). If not, they are contraries. He then gives the definition of contraries: they are two opposites such that neither is the negation of the other, and the intellection of each does not depend on the other. This definition excludes correlatives, which do involve such dependence. The gloss clarifies that the phrase 'except in relation to the other' in defining correlatives means that the relation is essential to the quiddity, not merely a necessary concomitant, so in correlatives intellection of each requires intellection of the other, hence contraries are defined negatively with respect to that dependence.

Key Terms
المتقابلان
المتضايفان
الضدان
الضدان المشهوريان
تعقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That contraries always involve a lack of mutual dependence in a strict sense; rather, the definition excludes the specific kind of dependence found in correlatives.
Correction: Contraries are defined negatively with respect to the dependence characteristic of correlatives. They may still have other logical relations, but they do not require intellection of the other for their own intellection.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes contraries from correlatives?
A) Contraries can be negations of each other.
B) The intellection of each contrary does not depend on intellection of the other.
C) Contraries always have a middle state.
D) Correlatives are a subclass of contraries.

Key Takeaway
The primary division of opposites is into correlatives, which are understood only in relation to each other, and contraries, which are not so dependent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson teaches that some concepts are only understood in relation to others. What does this imply about your understanding of Allah? Is your knowledge of Him always tainted by creation, or can you have a direct intellection? How does that affect your worship?
?
Consider a strong disagreement you have with someone about religion. Is it a correlative opposition (each view depends on the other) or a contrary opposition (both stand independently)? How might changing your frame of opposition lead to better dialogue and closer to truth?

Real Contraries Require Utmost Difference
The author introduces the condition of utmost difference for real contraries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why real contraries in kalam require utmost difference and how they differ from other opposites.

Core Idea
Only opposites with maximum difference are real contraries.

In kalam, not all opposites are equal. Some are mere mutual opponents, while others—like black and white—are real contraries because they stand at the extremes. This distinction matters for understanding how attributes are related in divine science.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author defines the condition.
Pay attention to the terms 'ghāyat al-khilāf' and 'al-mutaʿānidān'.
2
Identify the condition
Find the phrase that states the requirement for real contraries: utmost difference and distance.
Contrast it with the example of red and yellow.
3
Compare the examples
See why black and white are real contraries but red and yellow are not.
Think about how this applies to attributes like knowledge and ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يشترط في الضدين أن يكون بينهما غاية الخلاف والبعد كالسواد والبياض فإنهما متخالفان متباعدان في الغاية، ( دون الحمرة والصفرة إذ ليس بينهما ولا بين أحدهما وبين السواد والبياض ذلك الخلاف والتباعد فيسميان بالمتعاندين والضدان بهذا المعنى يسميان بالحقيقيين. قوله : ( بالحقيقيين) لكونه المعتبر في العلوم الحقيقية، كذا قال الشيخ.
And it is sometimes stipulated regarding the two contraries that there be between them utmost difference and distance, like black and white, for they are differing and far apart to the utmost, unlike red and yellow, for there is not between them nor between one of them and black and white that difference and distance, so they are called mutual opponents (al-mutaʿānidān). And the two contraries in this sense are called the real contraries (al-ḍiddān al-ḥaqīqiyyān). His saying: 'the real contraries'—because they are what is considered in the real sciences, thus said the Shaykh.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage introduces a stricter definition of contraries used in the real sciences: they require utmost difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) and distance, like black and white. Opposites that lack this extreme opposition, like red and yellow, are called mutual opponents (al-mutaʿānidān). The term 'real contraries' (al-ḍiddān al-ḥaqīqiyyān) is reserved for the former, as they are the ones considered in scientific discourse. The gloss confirms that this is the usage of the Shaykh (al-Rāzī or al-Ījī).

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all opposites are real contraries in the strict sense.
Correction: Only opposites with utmost difference are considered real contraries in kalam; others are merely mutual opponents.

Checkpoint

A) They are not opposites.
B) They lack utmost difference.
C) They are correlatives.
D) They require a middle state.

Key Takeaway
In kalam, real contraries require utmost difference, like black and white, not mere opposition.

Inclusion of Mutual Opponents in the Division of Opposites
The author discusses the inclusion of mutual opponents in the fourfold division of opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the classification of opposites depends on the definition of contrariety, and how mutual opponents fit differently under popular vs. real contrariety.

Core Idea
The fourfold division of opposites is not fixed—it hinges on which notion of contrariety you adopt.

The standard classification of opposites into four categories seems straightforward, but Islamic philosophers debate whether mutual opponents (like black and white, or red and yellow) belong under contrariety or form a separate category. This passage clarifies that the answer depends on whether we use the popular or real meaning of contrariety. Understanding this nuance prevents us from assuming a rigid system.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own structure come through.
Notice the conditional starting with 'فإن اعتبر'.
2
Identify the two definitions of contrariety
The passage introduces 'popular contrariety' and 'real contrariety'. Find how they are defined and why they are named so.
3
See how mutual opponents are treated under each definition
Under popular contrariety, mutual opponents are included in the fourfold division; under real contrariety, they become a fifth division.
This is the key point.
4
Note the extra nuance from the Shaykh
The passage adds that the Shaykh (al-Rāzī) required utmost difference even in popular contrariety, which can exclude some pairs from all four divisions.
Also note that popular contrariety can involve non-existential opposites, distinguishing it from privation-possession and affirmation-negation.
5
Reflect on why this matters
Consider how this shows that classifications in logic and philosophy can depend on definitions, not just fixed categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن اعتبر في تقسيم المتقابلين إلى الأقسام الأربعة التضاد المشهوري الشامل للتعاند ، فذاك، وإن اعتبر الحقيقي وجب جعل المتعاندين قسماً خامساً . قوله : ( التضاد المشهوري الشامل إلخ) يسمى هذا التضاد بالمشهوري؛ لكونه المشهور فيما بين عوام الفلاسفة، ويسمى المعنى الخاص بالتضاد الحقيقي؛ لكونه المعتبر في علومهم الحقيقية. وقد يقال : الشيخ صرح باشتراط غاية الخلاف في التضاد المشهوري أيضا، وحينئذ يكون تقابل مثل السواد والصفرة خارجاً عن الأقسام الأربعة البتة. وصرح أيضاً بأن الضدين في التضاد المشهوري لا يلزم أن يكونا موجودين، بل قد يكون أحدهما عدماً للآخر فهو لا يكون قسيماً لتقابل العدم والملكة وتقابل السلب والإيجاب.
If the consideration in the division of opposites into four divisions is the popular contrariety which includes mutual opposition (al-taʿānd), then that is fine; but if the real contrariety is considered, then it is necessary to make the mutual opponents a fifth division. His saying: 'the popular contrariety that includes [mutual opposition]'—this contrariety is called popular because it is what is famous among the common philosophers, and the specific meaning is called real contrariety because it is what is considered in their real sciences. And it is said: The Shaykh explicitly stipulated utmost difference even in popular contrariety as well, and in that case the opposition like that between red and yellow falls entirely outside the four divisions. And he also explicitly stated that the two contraries in popular contrariety do not necessarily have to exist, but one of them may be a non-existence of the other; so it is not a counterpart to the opposition of privation and possession (al-ʿadam wa al-malaka) nor to the opposition of affirmation and negation (al-salb wa al-ījāb).
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage addresses a classification issue: the standard fourfold division of opposites (correlatives, contraries, privation/possession, affirmation/negation) assumes the popular notion of contrariety, which includes mutual opponents like red and yellow. If one adopts the stricter real contrariety (with utmost difference), then mutual opponents become a fifth division. The gloss adds nuance: the Shaykh (al-Rāzī) may have required utmost difference even for popular contrariety, which would exclude cases like red vs. yellow from all four divisions. Furthermore, in popular contrariety, one opposite can be non-existential, so it does not coincide with privation-possession or affirmation-negation. Thus the division of opposites is not straightforward and depends on the intended meaning of contrariety.

Key Terms
التضاد المشهوري
التضاد الحقيقي
التعاند
تقسيم المتقابلين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the fourfold division of opposites is fixed and absolute.
Correction: The passage shows that the division depends on whether we use popular or real contrariety; mutual opponents can be included or separate, and some pairs may even fall outside all divisions.

Checkpoint
If real contrariety is used, how do mutual opponents fit into the division of opposites?
A) They are included in popular contrariety.
B) They become a fifth division.
C) They are considered a type of correlatives.
D) They are excluded from all divisions.

Key Takeaway
The classification of opposites depends on whether we adopt popular or real contrariety, making mutual opponents either included or separate.

Luzūm and Khulū: Inherence and Absence of Opposites in a Subject
The author classifies opposites by whether one necessarily inheres in the subject or not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four possibilities for how opposites relate to a subject: necessary inherence (specific or non-specific) and absence (with or without a middle state).

Core Idea
Opposites can inhere necessarily, or the subject can be free from both—with or without a middle.

This passage from the philosophers offers a precise classification of how opposites behave in a subject (mahall). It moves from necessary inherence (like whiteness in snow) to cases where neither opposite is present, either with a middle state (like lukewarm) or without any middle (like transparent). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for theological discussions about divine attributes and created substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the author's systematic style.
2
Identify the four cases
The passage describes four possibilities. List them: (1) one opposite necessarily inheres specifically, (2) one opposite necessarily inheres non-specifically, (3) subject free from both with a middle, (4) subject free from both without a middle.
3
Examine the examples
Look at each example: whiteness in snow, motion/rest in body, lukewarm as a middle, transparent as no middle. Understand why each fits.
4
Clarify the celestial sphere example
The passage addresses a potential misunderstanding: saying the sphere is neither heavy nor light does not imply a middle state; it means it is free from both.
5
Connect to kalam
Reflect on why this classification matters in theology— for instance, discussing God's attributes or the nature of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : وقد يلزم أحدهما المحل إما بعينه كالبياض اللازم للثلج، أو لا بعينه كالحركة والسكون للجسم، فإنه لا يخلو عنهما معاً، فأحدهما لا بعينه لازم له، وقد يخلو المحل عنهما معاً فلا لزوم هناك لأحدهما أصلاً . إما مع اتصافه بوسط بين المتضادين ويعبر عنه إما باسم وجودي كالمز المتوسط بين الحلو والحامض، وكالفاتر المتوسط بين الحار والبارد أو بسلب الطرفين كما يقال : لا عادل ولا جائر لمن اتصف بحالة متوسطة بين العدل والجور . وأما قولهم الفلك لا ثقيل ولا خفيف فلم يريدوا بسلب الطرفين هناك إثبات متوسطة بين الثقل والخفة أو دونه فيخلو المحل عن الوسط أيضاً ، كالشفاف الخالي عن السواد والبياض، وعن كل ما بتوسطهما من الألوان.
They (the philosophers) said: And one of the two opposites may be necessarily inherent in the subject, either specifically, like whiteness necessarily inhering in snow, or not specifically, like motion and rest in a body, for it is not free from both together, so one of them not specifically is necessary for it. And the subject may be free from both together, so there is no inherence of either at all. Either with the subject being qualified by a middle state between the two contraries—and that middle state is expressed either by a positive term, like the intermediate taste (al-muzz) between sweet and sour, and like the lukewarm (al-fātir) intermediate between hot and cold; or by negating the two extremes, as one says 'neither just nor unjust' of one described by an intermediate state between justice and injustice. As for their saying 'the celestial sphere is neither heavy nor light', they do not intend by negating the two extremes there to affirm a middle state between heaviness and lightness. Or without that (i.e., without being qualified by a middle state), so the subject is free also from the middle state, like the transparent (al-shaffāf) which is free from black and white and from all colors that mediate between them.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage classifies opposites with respect to their inherence in a subject (mahall). The philosophers (al-ḥukamā') note four possibilities: (1) One opposite necessarily inheres in the subject specifically (e.g., whiteness in snow). (2) One opposite necessarily inheres but not specifically, such that the subject is never free from both together, but any particular one is not fixed (e.g., a body is always either in motion or at rest, but a specific motion is not necessary). (3) The subject can be free from both opposites, either (a) with a middle state expressed positively (e.g., lukewarm between hot and cold) or by negation of extremes (e.g., 'neither just nor unjust'), or (b) without any middle state, like a transparent body having no color (neither black nor white nor any intermediate). The example of the celestial sphere being neither heavy nor light is clarified: it does not imply an intermediate state; it is simply free from both extremes. Thus the behavior of opposites in subjects varies widely, and the author systematically lays out the possibilities.

Key Terms
لزوم
المحل
الوسط
الشفاف
المز
الفاتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a subject is free from both opposites, it must have a middle state.
Correction: The transparent example shows a subject free from black, white, and all intermediate colors, so no middle state exists. Similarly, the celestial sphere is neither heavy nor light without an intermediate.

Checkpoint
What does the example of 'transparent' (al-shaffāf) illustrate?
A) A subject with a middle state between black and white.
B) A subject free from both opposites and any middle state.
C) A subject in which one opposite necessarily inheres.
D) A subject that can have both opposites simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
The relationship of opposites to a subject can be divided into four cases: necessary inherence (specific or not) and absence (with or without a middle state).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage shows that a subject can be free from both opposites without any middle state, like a transparent body having no color. Reflect on a situation in your own life where you assumed there must be a middle ground between two extremes (e.g., truth and falsehood, right and wrong). Is it possible that you have been trying to find a comfortable middle where none exists? What would it mean for you to be 'transparent'—free from both extremes—in that area?
?
Consider the example of motion and rest necessarily inhering in a body, but not specifically. In your spiritual life, are there attributes that you think must always be present in you (e.g., patience, anger) but perhaps they are not always required? Or are there necessary attributes of faith that you may be neglecting? Ask yourself: 'Am I assuming that a certain quality must always inhere specifically, when in reality it may be required only at certain times?' How can you adjust your expectations accordingly?
?
The philosophers classify the inherence of opposites with precision. How often do you judge others or yourself using oversimplified dichotomies? For instance, do you label someone as 'just' or 'unjust' without considering that they might be in an intermediate state? Or worse, do you assume that if someone is not one extreme they must be the other? Use the categories from this lesson to examine a recent judgment you made. How would the philosophers' classification correct your thinking?

Succession of Opposites and the Condition of Rest
Classification of opposites regarding succession and condition of rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam concept of succession of opposites on a single subject and the condition of rest required for certain types of opposites.

Core Idea
Not all opposites can follow each other directly; some require a moment of rest between them.

In kalam, the concept of succession of opposites on a subject is important for understanding change and causality. This lesson examines a key classification: some opposites, like black and white, can directly replace each other at an instant, while others, like ascending and descending motions, require an intermediate state of rest. The passage clarifies this distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the author's own wording introduce the two types of opposites.
2
Identify the two types of opposites
Notice that the passage presents two cases: one where succession is possible (like black and white) and another where it is not without rest (like ascending and descending motions).
The key difference is the need for rest.
3
Understand the condition of rest
Reflect on why rest is necessary for motion opposites. The commentary will explain that contrary motions cannot follow directly without a break.
This connects to the broader kalam discussion of change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً قد يمكن تعاقبهما على المحل، كالسواد والبياض) بحيث لا يخلو عنهما معاً، بل يعدم أحدهما عنه، ويوجد الآخر فيه في آن واحد كالسواد والبياض ( أو لا يمكن تعاقبهما على المحل بحيث لا يخلو عنهما ، ( كالحركتين الصاعدة والهابطة)، فإنه لا يجوز تعاقبهما على محل واحد ( إن قلنا : ( يجب أن يكون ( بينهما سكون ) ، كما هو المشهور.
And also, it may be possible for the two opposites to succeed each other on the subject, like black and white, such that the subject is not free from both together; rather, one is removed from it and the other comes to exist in it at a single instant, like black and white. Or it may not be possible for them to succeed each other on the subject such that it is not free from both together, like the two motions, ascending and descending, for it is not permissible for them to succeed each other on a single subject if we say that there must be rest between them, as is well-known.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage treats the possibility of succession (taʿāqub) of opposites on a single subject. Some opposites can succeed each other directly, like black and white: a surface can become black after being white without an intermediate state, but it is never simultaneously both. For other opposites, like ascending and descending motions, succession is not possible without an intermediate state of rest (sukūn) between them, according to the well-known view. The reason is that a body cannot directly change from upward to downward motion without a moment of rest, because these are contrary motions and cannot be consecutive without a break. Thus the condition of succession depends on the nature of the opposites.

Key Terms
تعاقب
الحركتين الصاعدة والهابطة
السكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All opposites can succeed each other directly on a subject.
Correction: Not all opposites can succeed directly; motion opposites like ascending and descending require a state of rest between them.

Checkpoint
Why, according to the well-known view, cannot ascending and descending motions succeed each other directly?
A) They are not opposites.
B) They require a state of rest between them.
C) They cannot exist in the same subject.
D) They are not contraries in the real sense.

Key Takeaway
The succession of opposites on a subject is not uniform; motion opposites require an intermediate state of rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that some opposites require rest while others can flip instantly. Think of a spiritual or moral 'opposite' you struggle with—like patience and anger. Have you ever tried to replace anger with patience in an instant, without a moment of conscious pause? Did it work? Where in your life do you need to deliberately insert a rest—a pause for reflection—before attempting change?
?
The succession of black and white happens at an instant, yet ascending and descending motions demand rest. Consider your own transitions between states of faith (iman) and heedlessness (ghaflah). Do you treat them like black and white—expecting an instant flip—or do you acknowledge the need for a transitional rest? What would that rest look like for you in practice?

Contrariety Exists Only Between Species of the Same Genus
The author states the general rule that contrariety is restricted to species of a single genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that contrariety (tadḍād) is a relation that holds only between species that belong to the same genus.

Core Idea
Contrariety is intra-generic, not inter-generic.

This brief passage from a classical kalam text establishes a foundational rule about logical opposition. It may seem simple, but it is crucial for correctly classifying opposites and avoiding confusion. Let's read the author's statement and unpack its meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own wording sink in before interpreting.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the Arabic words for 'contrariety', 'species', and 'genus' in the passage. Note them down.
These terms are: التضاد, أنواع, جنس, الأجناس.
3
Understand the rule
The passage states that contrariety does not occur except between species of one genus. That means contrariety is a relation within a single genus.
4
Consider examples
Think of examples: black and white are contrary because both are colors (same genus). Hot and cold are contrary because both are temperatures. But there is no contrariety between a color and a sound because they belong to different genera.
5
Avoid the misconception
Do not think that any two different things are contraries. They must be species of the same genus. The author explicitly denies contrariety between genera.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن التضاد لا يكون إلا بين أنواع جنس واحد، أي لا تضاد بين الأجناس أصلاً، ولا بين
And know that contrariety does not occur except between species of one genus, that is, there is no contrariety between genera at all, nor between
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This very brief passage states a fundamental rule: contrariety (opposition as conceived here) exists only between species that fall under a single genus. For example, black and white are both colors (genus), so they are contrary. But there is no contrariety between different genera (e.g., color and sound) or between a species and its genus (e.g., color and whiteness). The statement is cut off, but the intended meaning is clear: the domain of contrariety is intra-generic. This rule is essential for understanding the logical classification of opposites in Aristotelian and post-Aristotelian philosophy.

Key Terms
التضاد
أنواع جنس واحد
الأجناس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two things that are different can be considered contraries.
Correction: Contrariety only occurs between species of the same genus, not between genera or between a genus and its species.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, where does contrariety occur?
A) Between any two different things.
B) Between species of the same genus.
C) Between different genera.
D) Between genus and species.

Key Takeaway
Contrariety is a relation that holds only between species that belong to the same genus, not between genera or between genus and species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a current disagreement you consider a clear opposition. What is the genus that both sides belong to? How does seeing this change your approach? Commit to a specific action within 24 hours.
?
In your spiritual life, have you ever treated two duties (e.g., prayer and seeking knowledge) as contraries? According to this lesson, what genus unites them? How can you integrate them instead? Write a short du'a asking for help to see beyond false dichotomies.
?
The rule says contrariety is only within a genus. Where in your life have you assumed a necessary opposition where there is only difference? Name one area and state a concrete step to step out of that binary.
